# Mississippi Building Codes
## **Mississippi Building Codes**
Mississippi building codes are tailored to address the state’s hot, humid climate, hurricane risks in coastal regions, and local infrastructure challenges. Emphasis is placed on structural integrity, fire safety, electrical reliability, and energy efficiency, particularly in regions vulnerable to high winds and flooding.
## **Current Commercial Code of Mississippi**
**Base Code:** 2021 International Building Code (IBC)  
**Effective Date:** 2024  
**Amendments:** Mississippi adopts the 2021 IBC with state-specific amendments. These adjustments focus on improving safety in hurricane-prone zones, especially in the Gulf Coast region, and ensuring structural resilience for both low-rise and high-rise commercial buildings.
### **Other Relevant Codes for Commercial Buildings in Mississippi:**
  * **Mississippi Fire Code:** Based on the 2021 International Fire Code (IFC), with tailored amendments for emergency response and fire suppression.
  * **Mississippi Mechanical Code:** Incorporates provisions from the 2021 International Mechanical Code (IMC).
  * **Mississippi Plumbing Code:** Based on the 2021 International Plumbing Code (IPC).
  * **Mississippi Electrical Code:** Based on the 2023 National Electrical Code (NEC), with state-specific amendments for safety and reliability.
  * **Mississippi Elevator and Escalator Code:** Adopts ASME A17.1-2019 standards for conveyance safety.


### **Energy Compliance:**
  * **Mississippi Energy Code:** Currently references the 2021 International Energy Conservation Code (IECC) for both commercial and residential buildings. Local amendments emphasize HVAC efficiency and insulation in high humidity zones.


## **Current Residential Code of Mississippi**
!Mississippi
**Base Code:** 2021 International Residential Code (IRC)  
**Effective Date:** 2024  
**Amendments:** Mississippi applies specific amendments to address resilience against hurricanes, wind uplift, and flood risks, particularly along the Gulf Coast. The code covers design and construction for one- and two-family dwellings and townhouses.
#### **Other Relevant Residential Building Codes in Mississippi:**
  * **Mississippi Plumbing Code:** Based on the 2021 IPC, with minor adjustments.
  * **Mississippi Mechanical Code:** Adopts the 2021 IMC.
  * **Mississippi Electrical Code:** Follows the 2023 NEC, ensuring residential electrical safety and surge protection.
  * **Mississippi Fire Sprinkler Codes:**
    * NFPA 13 (2022) for standard systems
    * NFPA 13R (2022) for low-rise residential
    * NFPA 13D (2022) for one- and two-family dwellings


#### **Energy Compliance:**
  * **Mississippi Energy Code:** The state follows the 2021 IECC for residential buildings, amended to account for high summer cooling loads and moisture control.


#### **Accessibility:**
  * **Mississippi Accessibility Requirements:** Mississippi references ADA 2010 Standards and ICC A117.1, with localized guidance for public housing and educational facilities.


**Climate Zones:** 2A, 3A
* * *
## **List of All Current Codes in Mississippi**
  * Mississippi Building Code 2024 (IBC 2021) – View Code
  * Mississippi Residential Code 2024 (IRC 2021) – View Code
  * Mississippi Fire Code 2024 (IFC 2021) – View Code
  * Mississippi Electrical Code 2023 (NEC 2023) – View Code
  * Mississippi Fire Sprinkler Code 2022 (NFPA 13) – View Code
  * Mississippi One- and Two-Family Dwelling Sprinkler Code 2022 (NFPA 13D) – View Code
  * Mississippi Low-Rise Residential Fire Sprinkler Code 2022 (NFPA 13R) – View Code
  * Mississippi Fuel Gas Code 2022 (IFGC 2021) – View Code
  * Mississippi Elevator Code 2022 (ASME A17.1-2019) – View Code
  * Mississippi Concrete Code (ACI 318-10 and ACI 117-10) – View Code
  * Mississippi Post-Installed Anchors & Structural Components – View Code
  * Mississippi Standard for Residential Construction in High Wind Regions (NFPA 5000-2021) – View Code


## Adopted Building Codes by Category
Code Type | Adopted Version  
---|---  
International Building Code (IBC) | 2018  
International Residential Code (IRC) | 2018  
International Energy Conservation Code (IECC) | 2018  
International Existing Building Code (IEBC) | 2018  
International Fire Code (IFC) | 2018  
International Plumbing Code (IPC) | 2018 / 2015  
International Mechanical Code (IMC) | 2018  
International Fuel Gas Code (IFGC) | 2018  
International Swimming Pool and Spa Code (ISPSC) | 2015  
Private Sewage Disposal Code (IPSDC) | 2006  
## **Approved Compliance Tools**
  * **REScheck & COMcheck:** Accepted for energy code compliance under the 2021 IECC.
  * **Manual J, S, D:** Required for residential HVAC design.
  * **Structural Calculations:** Mandatory for wind-load compliance in Gulf Coast zones.


## Energy Code in Mississippi
For commercial and state-owned buildings, Mississippi enforces ASHRAE 90.1-2010. This requirement does not apply to residential structures unless specifically adopted by a local jurisdiction.
Some cities voluntarily follow the 2018 IECC to support energy-efficient construction, especially in larger metro areas or state-funded projects.
### Code Adoption and Change Process
#### Local Authority Adoption (Home Rule System)
Mississippi operates under a **home rule system** , meaning each **city or county has full authority** to adopt and enforce building codes independently. The **Mississippi State Codes Council** does **not mandate statewide adoption** of I-Codes (International Codes), but it does provide a list of approved code versions that local jurisdictions may choose from.
If a jurisdiction opts to enforce building regulations, the codes adopted must be **approved by the State Building Code Council** , typically from the latest editions of the **IBC** , **IRC** , **IMC** , **IFGC** , **IECC** , and others.
##### **Code Change Cycle**
Irregular – not on a fixed schedule. Adoption tends to follow ICC releases by 2–3 years, but varies depending on legislative and regulatory review.
There is **no fixed or statewide cycle** for code updates in Mississippi. The decision to update is made **locally** and can vary widely between jurisdictions.
In larger cities like **Jackson** or **Biloxi** , the code adoption process typically includes:
  * Review by local building departments or city councils
  * Public hearings or community input sessions
  * A formal vote to adopt the code (often with amendments)
  * A **transition period** of 6–12 months to allow stakeholders (builders, code officials, engineers) to adjust to the new regulations


##### Next Code Update
Tentatively expected after the release and review of the 2024 I-Codes (possibly in 2026).
* * *
#### History
  * **2024:** Adoption of 2021 IBC, IRC, IFC, and updated sprinkler and elevator codes.
  * **2023:** Mississippi Electrical Code updated to NEC 2023.
  * **2022:** Updates to NFPA 13, 13R, and 13D for sprinkler systems.
  * **2019–2021:** Incorporation of structural concrete (ACI) and mechanical codes.
  * **Earlier:** Local jurisdictions previously adopted codes independently; the statewide standardization process began consolidating under the Fire Marshal’s office.

# California Building Codes
## Current Commercial Code of California
!California
The current Commercial Building Code of California, as outlined in the 2022 edition of the California Building Standards Code (Title 24), includes comprehensive regulations for commercial structures regarding safety, accessibility, and sustainability. The code incorporates amended versions of the International Building Code 2021 (IBC 2021), covering diverse areas such as fire safety, mechanical, plumbing, energy, and electrical systems. Specific amendments to the 2021 IBC address regional requirements and enhancements, ensuring that commercial buildings adhere to high safety and efficiency standards, reflecting California’s specific needs related to seismic activity, climate, and technological advancements. These standards are enforceable by state and local building departments to maintain consistency across the state.
Read More: Los Angeles City Building Codes 
## List of all California Building Codes
The current Residential Code of California, as specified in the 2022 California Residential Code, modifies the 2021 International Residential Code (IRC 2021) to tailor it to the state’s unique requirements. This code governs the construction, alteration, movement, enlargement, replacement, repair, equipment, use and occupancy, location, maintenance, removal, and demolition of detached one- and two-family dwellings and townhouses not more than three stories in height. The amendments made to the standard IRC reflect California’s specific needs concerning seismic safety, fire hazards, and environmental conditions, ensuring that residential buildings provide safe, sustainable, and efficient living spaces. These regulations are mandatory and enforceable across all jurisdictions within the state to maintain uniformity and safety in residential construction.

California Building Climate Zone Areas
* * *
#### Code Adoption and Change Process
##### Code Change Process
The California Energy Commission (CEC) is authorized to establish building energy efficiency requirements, both the California Energy Code and CALGreen. CEC is responsible for overseeing the public process and rulemaking proceeding.
##### Code Change Cycle
California’s Building Energy Efficiency Standards are updated on an approximately three-year cycle.
##### Next Code Update
* * *
#### History
[Information on previous California energy standards is available here](http://www.energy.ca.gov/title24/standards_archive/).
**January 1, 2017** | 2016 Building Energy Efficiency Standards Go Into Effect | The 2016 Building Energy Efficiency Standards become effective, introducing enhanced measures for energy conservation in new building constructions. Read the press release here.  
---|---|---  
**September 4, 2015** | Publication of the Existing Buildings Energy Efficiency Action Plan | The California Energy Commission publishes the Existing Buildings Energy Efficiency Action Plan, outlining strategies to improve energy efficiency in existing buildings across the state.  
**July 1, 2014** | 2013 Standards Become Effective | The 2013 Building Energy Efficiency Standards take effect, expected to achieve 25% more energy savings compared to the 2008 Standards. These include advanced measures for both commercial and residential buildings, surpassing the efficiency levels of ASHRAE 90.1-2010 and the 2012 IECC respectively.  
**January 23, 2013** | Approval of 2013 Standards for Publication | The Building Standards Commission approves the 2013 Standards for publication, setting the stage for their implementation the following year.  
**May 24, 2012** | Editorial Advocacy for Energy Code Update | The San Francisco Chronicle publishes an editorial titled “A Green Future Starts at Home for Californians,” advocating for the update to California’s state building energy code (Title 24, Part 6), highlighting widespread support from various stakeholders.  
**2012** | Preparation and Approval of 2013 Building Energy Efficiency Standards | The California Energy Commission prepares and approves updates to the state building energy code (Title 24, Part 6), aiming for significant energy savings over previous standards, with public comments and hearings facilitating the process. View the slide presentation on the 2013 Building Energy Efficiency Standards.  
**February 2011** | Ground-Breaking Green Building Legislation in San Francisco | San Francisco passes groundbreaking legislation requiring non-residential building owners to benchmark and publicly disclose energy use, with future mandates for energy audits in larger commercial properties.  
**January 12, 2010** | Adoption of CALGREEN | California adopts the nation’s first mandatory green building standards, CALGREEN, effective January 1, 2011. This code mandates reductions in water use, waste diversion, and the use of low-emitting materials, among other environmental standards.  
**January 1, 2010** | Effectiveness of 2008 Standards | The 2008 Building Energy Efficiency Standards become effective, showing an average energy performance improvement of at least 21% over ASHRAE 90.1-2004.  
**October 11, 2009** | Legislative Developments in Building Energy Efficiency | Assembly Bill No. 758 is approved, mandating the California Energy Commission to develop a comprehensive program for energy savings in existing building stock, with the PUC investigating energy efficiency financing options.  
**September 2009** | $3.1 Billion Energy Efficiency Budget Approved | The California Public Utilities Commission approves a three-year $3.1 billion energy efficiency budget aimed at retrofitting residential buildings, making public buildings more energy efficient, and promoting zero net energy homes and commercial buildings. Source  
**February 2, 2009** | Zero Net Energy Bill Introduced | A bill is introduced in the state assembly mandating “zero net energy” standards for residential buildings starting in 2020, though it does not pass.  
**December 14, 2004** | Green Building Initiative by Governor Schwarzenegger | Governor Arnold Schwarzenegger issues Executive Order S-20-04, directing the California Energy Commission to enhance energy efficiency in nonresidential buildings by 20% by 2015 as part of the Green Building Initiative.  
**1974-1975** | Establishment of the California Energy Commission | Following the Warren-Alquist Act, the California Energy Commission is established, tasked with creating energy efficiency requirements for buildings, initially setting statewide energy standards in 1975 for low-rise residential buildings by the Department of Housing and Community Development.

Cosimina

Her involvement spans across advocacy, technical guidance, outreach programs, and the formation of strategic coalitions.

# Code Status Maps
##### BCAP has created a series of maps to provide a national snapshot of building energy code adoption and implementation status. Click on a map to learn more about the status of energy codes in each state.
### Commercial Adoption
States that have adopted a statewide commercial energy code that meets or exceeds an ASHRAE 90.1 standard. This map reflects only mandatory statewide commercial energy codes currently in effect.
![](https://www.bcapcodes.org/code-status/residential/)
### Residential Adoption
States that have adopted a statewide residential energy code that meets or exceeds an IECC model code. This map reflects only mandatory statewide residential energy codes currently in effect.

# BCAP Newsletter
Welcome to the BCAP newsletter archives! Our **Building Energy Codes _Connection_** Newsletter, published quarterly, is filled with resources to keep you up-to-date with our work, national and regional news, upcoming events, and more. Each edition includes recent stories and publications and features the accomplishments of other energy efficiency organizations. We’re always looking for news stories. If you have information about a story you think we should cover, please send your suggestions to info@bcapcodes.org.

# Sodium-Ion Batteries vs Lithium Ion Batteries: A Comparative Analysis
Posted on December 10, 2024 by Maureen Guttman
!sodium ion batteries vs lithium ion batteries
Sodium-ion vs. Lithium-ion Battery: Which is the Better Alternative? Sodium is more than 500 times more abundant than lithium, which is available in only a few countries. Sodium-ion batteries charge faster than lithium-ion variants and have a three times higher lifecycle. However, sodium-ion batteries lack a well-established raw material supply chain, and the technology is still in the early stages of development. ##### Current Residential Code
**2015 Alabama Residential Building Code**  
Based on the 2015 International Residential Code (IRC) with Alabama-specific amendments  
Effective 10/1/2016
**2015 Alabama Residential Energy Code**  
Based on the 2015 International Energy Conservation Code (IECC) with Alabama-specific amendments  
Effective 10/1/2016
**R406.4 (N1106.4) ERI-based compliance.**  
Compliance based on an ERI analysis requires that the rated design be shown to have an ERI less than or equal to a score of 70 in both zones 2 and 3 when compared to the ERI reference design.
RES _check_ is applicable by county or jurisdiction.
!albama building codes
**Climate Zones:** 2A, 3A
* * *
#### Code Adoption and Change Process
##### Code Change Process
In 2010, Senate Bill 315 became law and gave the Alabama Energy and Residential Codes (AERC) Board the authority to adopt mandatory residential and commercial energy codes for the entire state, applicable to new construction and substantial renovations. This act also gave the AERC Board authority to adopt residential building codes for any jurisdictions that had not implemented a code prior to March 2010. Individual cities and jurisdictions are allowed to adopt energy codes that exceed statewide standards. Above code programs are permitted upon approval by the Alabama Residential and Energy Codes Board.
##### Code Change Cycle
No set schedule. SB 315 did not create an automatic update cycle. The AERC Board is required to meet at least once per calendar year.
##### Next Code Update
Unknown
_Back to top_
* * *
#### History
[Alabama Energy and Residential Codes (AERC) Summary](http://www.adeca.alabama.gov/Divisions/energy/Documents/Codes/Summary%20of%20the%20AERC.pdf) | [Full code](http://adeca.alabama.gov/Divisions/energy/Documents/Codes/Alabama%20Energy%20and%20Residential%20Code%20\(updated%203-19-14\).pdf)  
---  
| **May 2006** | Executive Order 33 Issued | Governor of Alabama mandates state departments and agencies to encourage and promote energy conservation in state-owned buildings.  
---|---|---  
**January 1, 2005** | Adoption of ABECC | The Alabama Building Commission adopts the 2004 Alabama Building Energy Conservation Code (ABECC) for state-funded buildings, effective until June 1, 2009.  
**January 1, 2008** | ADECA Roadmap Publication | ADECA publishes a roadmap for cities, towns, and counties in Alabama to adopt the 2006 IECC.  
**December 2008** | Adoption of the 2006 IECC | Alabama adopts the 2006 IECC as the standard in new public buildings. Agencies are required to purchase Energy Star equipment when cost-effective. The 2006 IECC is encouraged for private commercial construction.  
**March 9, 2010** | SB 315 Signed into Law | Governor Bob Riley signs SB 315, creating the Alabama Energy and Residential Codes Board with authority over the state’s mandatory residential and commercial energy codes and residential building codes for jurisdictions without prior codes.  
**April 12, 2011** | Residential Energy Code Proposal Approved | Alabama Residential and Energy Codes Board approves a new residential energy code based on the 2009 IRC with amendments to make it equivalent to the 2009 IECC. The new commercial code based on the 2009 IECC had been approved on January 25th.  
**November 15, 2011** | Executive Order Number 25 Issued | Governor Robert Bentley issues an order to extend energy standards for state agency buildings, targeting a 30% reduction in energy consumption by Fiscal Year 2015 relative to Fiscal Year 2005 levels.  
**October 1, 2012** | AERC Becomes Effective | The first edition of the Alabama Residential and Energy Code (AERC) becomes effective statewide, allowing local jurisdictions to adopt more stringent energy codes.  
**April 17, 2014** | AERC Board Meeting | The board meets to discuss the 2012 IRC and IECC. They decide not to adopt the 2012 IECC due to the recent implementation of the first statewide code. They support considering the 2015 IECC after its release.  
**January 1, 2014** | Duct Leakage Testing Requirement | Duct leakage testing becomes mandatory as per the energy code adopted in October 2012.  
**July 1, 2013** | R-8 Duct Insulation Requirement | R-8 duct insulation requirements come into effect, although initially set to start in 2013, they were part of the broader energy code considerations from previous years.  
**2021** | Adoption of 2021 International Codes | Alabama adopts the 2021 editions of the International Codes (including IBC, IRC, IECC, etc.), enhancing building safety and energy efficiency standards across the state.  
_Back to top_

# Mississippi Building Codes
## **Mississippi Building Codes**
Mississippi building codes are tailored to address the state’s hot, humid climate, hurricane risks in coastal regions, and local infrastructure challenges. Emphasis is placed on structural integrity, fire safety, electrical reliability, and energy efficiency, particularly in regions vulnerable to high winds and flooding.
## **Current Commercial Code of Mississippi**
**Base Code:** 2021 International Building Code (IBC)  
**Effective Date:** 2024  
**Amendments:** Mississippi adopts the 2021 IBC with state-specific amendments. These adjustments focus on improving safety in hurricane-prone zones, especially in the Gulf Coast region, and ensuring structural resilience for both low-rise and high-rise commercial buildings.
### **Other Relevant Codes for Commercial Buildings in Mississippi:**
  * **Mississippi Fire Code:** Based on the 2021 International Fire Code (IFC), with tailored amendments for emergency response and fire suppression.
  * **Mississippi Mechanical Code:** Incorporates provisions from the 2021 International Mechanical Code (IMC).
  * **Mississippi Plumbing Code:** Based on the 2021 International Plumbing Code (IPC).
  * **Mississippi Electrical Code:** Based on the 2023 National Electrical Code (NEC), with state-specific amendments for safety and reliability.
  * **Mississippi Elevator and Escalator Code:** Adopts ASME A17.1-2019 standards for conveyance safety.


### **Energy Compliance:**
  * **Mississippi Energy Code:** Currently references the 2021 International Energy Conservation Code (IECC) for both commercial and residential buildings. Local amendments emphasize HVAC efficiency and insulation in high humidity zones.


## **Current Residential Code of Mississippi**
!Mississippi
**Base Code:** 2021 International Residential Code (IRC)  
**Effective Date:** 2024  
**Amendments:** Mississippi applies specific amendments to address resilience against hurricanes, wind uplift, and flood risks, particularly along the Gulf Coast. The code covers design and construction for one- and two-family dwellings and townhouses.
#### **Other Relevant Residential Building Codes in Mississippi:**
  * **Mississippi Plumbing Code:** Based on the 2021 IPC, with minor adjustments.
  * **Mississippi Mechanical Code:** Adopts the 2021 IMC.
  * **Mississippi Electrical Code:** Follows the 2023 NEC, ensuring residential electrical safety and surge protection.
  * **Mississippi Fire Sprinkler Codes:**
    * NFPA 13 (2022) for standard systems
    * NFPA 13R (2022) for low-rise residential
    * NFPA 13D (2022) for one- and two-family dwellings


#### **Energy Compliance:**
  * **Mississippi Energy Code:** The state follows the 2021 IECC for residential buildings, amended to account for high summer cooling loads and moisture control.


#### **Accessibility:**
  * **Mississippi Accessibility Requirements:** Mississippi references ADA 2010 Standards and ICC A117.1, with localized guidance for public housing and educational facilities.


**Climate Zones:** 2A, 3A
* * *
## **List of All Current Codes in Mississippi**
  * Mississippi Building Code 2024 (IBC 2021) – View Code
  * Mississippi Residential Code 2024 (IRC 2021) – View Code
  * Mississippi Fire Code 2024 (IFC 2021) – View Code
  * Mississippi Electrical Code 2023 (NEC 2023) – View Code
  * Mississippi Fire Sprinkler Code 2022 (NFPA 13) – View Code
  * Mississippi One- and Two-Family Dwelling Sprinkler Code 2022 (NFPA 13D) – View Code
  * Mississippi Low-Rise Residential Fire Sprinkler Code 2022 (NFPA 13R) – View Code
  * Mississippi Fuel Gas Code 2022 (IFGC 2021) – View Code
  * Mississippi Elevator Code 2022 (ASME A17.1-2019) – View Code
  * Mississippi Concrete Code (ACI 318-10 and ACI 117-10) – View Code
  * Mississippi Post-Installed Anchors & Structural Components – View Code
  * Mississippi Standard for Residential Construction in High Wind Regions (NFPA 5000-2021) – View Code


## Adopted Building Codes by Category
Code Type | Adopted Version  
---|---  
International Building Code (IBC) | 2018  
International Residential Code (IRC) | 2018  
International Energy Conservation Code (IECC) | 2018  
International Existing Building Code (IEBC) | 2018  
International Fire Code (IFC) | 2018  
International Plumbing Code (IPC) | 2018 / 2015  
International Mechanical Code (IMC) | 2018  
International Fuel Gas Code (IFGC) | 2018  
International Swimming Pool and Spa Code (ISPSC) | 2015  
Private Sewage Disposal Code (IPSDC) | 2006  
## **Approved Compliance Tools**
  * **REScheck & COMcheck:** Accepted for energy code compliance under the 2021 IECC.
  * **Manual J, S, D:** Required for residential HVAC design.
  * **Structural Calculations:** Mandatory for wind-load compliance in Gulf Coast zones.


## Energy Code in Mississippi
For commercial and state-owned buildings, Mississippi enforces ASHRAE 90.1-2010. This requirement does not apply to residential structures unless specifically adopted by a local jurisdiction.
Some cities voluntarily follow the 2018 IECC to support energy-efficient construction, especially in larger metro areas or state-funded projects.
### Code Adoption and Change Process
#### Local Authority Adoption (Home Rule System)
Mississippi operates under a **home rule system** , meaning each **city or county has full authority** to adopt and enforce building codes independently. The **Mississippi State Codes Council** does **not mandate statewide adoption** of I-Codes (International Codes), but it does provide a list of approved code versions that local jurisdictions may choose from.
If a jurisdiction opts to enforce building regulations, the codes adopted must be **approved by the State Building Code Council** , typically from the latest editions of the **IBC** , **IRC** , **IMC** , **IFGC** , **IECC** , and others.
##### **Code Change Cycle**
Irregular – not on a fixed schedule. Adoption tends to follow ICC releases by 2–3 years, but varies depending on legislative and regulatory review.
There is **no fixed or statewide cycle** for code updates in Mississippi. The decision to update is made **locally** and can vary widely between jurisdictions.
In larger cities like **Jackson** or **Biloxi** , the code adoption process typically includes:
  * Review by local building departments or city councils
  * Public hearings or community input sessions
  * A formal vote to adopt the code (often with amendments)
  * A **transition period** of 6–12 months to allow stakeholders (builders, code officials, engineers) to adjust to the new regulations


##### Next Code Update
Tentatively expected after the release and review of the 2024 I-Codes (possibly in 2026).
* * *
#### History
  * **2024:** Adoption of 2021 IBC, IRC, IFC, and updated sprinkler and elevator codes.
  * **2023:** Mississippi Electrical Code updated to NEC 2023.
  * **2022:** Updates to NFPA 13, 13R, and 13D for sprinkler systems.
  * **2019–2021:** Incorporation of structural concrete (ACI) and mechanical codes.
  * **Earlier:** Local jurisdictions previously adopted codes independently; the statewide standardization process began consolidating under the Fire Marshal’s office.


* * *

# Mississippi Electrical Code 2023
## **Mississippi Electrical Code 2023**
**Base Code:** 2023 National Electrical Code (NEC 2023)
Discover more
Electrical wiring
electrical installations
wiring
**Effective Date:** January 1, 2023
**Adopting Authority:** Mississippi State Fire Marshal’s Office
**Applies To:** All electrical installations in commercial, residential, industrial, and institutional buildings statewide
### **Overview**
The **Mississippi Electrical Code 2023** adopts the **National Electrical Code (NEC) 2023** in full, with minor jurisdictional modifications to address regional safety concerns. This code sets the **minimum safety standards for the design, installation, inspection, and maintenance of electrical systems** in all buildings throughout the state.
NEC 2023 introduces updated safety provisions for emerging technologies, including **electric vehicles (EVs)** , **energy storage systems** , **microgrids** , and **solar photovoltaics (PV)** —all increasingly relevant for Mississippi’s infrastructure modernization.
## **Scope and Administration**
The Electrical Code governs:
  * **Wiring methods** and materials
  * **Overcurrent protection** (breakers and fuses)
  * **Service entrance installations**
  * **Panelboard and subpanel layouts**
  * **Lighting and appliance circuits**
  * **Grounding and bonding**
  * **Energy storage systems (ESS)**
  * **EV charging infrastructure**


It applies to:
  * New construction
  * Renovations and retrofits
  * Additions and service upgrades
  * Agricultural and utility installations
  * Mobile homes and manufactured housing


## **Key Articles in NEC 2023 Adopted by Mississippi**
The NEC is structured by **Articles** , each covering a major topic. Below are the most relevant Articles enforced under the Mississippi Electrical Code 2023:
### **Article 90 – Introduction**
  * Explains the **purpose** of the code: protect people and property from electrical hazards.
  * Clarifies that NEC is not a design manual, but a minimum safety standard.


### **Chapter 1: General Requirements**
  * **Article 100 – Definitions:** Standardized definitions for all terms used in the NEC.
  * **Article 110 – Requirements for Electrical Installations:**
    * Working space around equipment
    * Clearances
    * Electrical system identification
    * Voltage limitations


### **Chapter 2: Wiring and Protection**
  * **Article 200 – Grounded Conductors**
  * **Article 210 – Branch Circuits:**
    * Receptacle spacing
    * GFCI (Ground-Fault Circuit Interrupter) and AFCI (Arc-Fault Circuit Interrupter) protections
  * **Article 215 – Feeders**
  * **Article 220 – Load Calculations**
  * **Article 230 – Services:**
    * Service entrance conductors and main disconnects
  * **Article 240 – Overcurrent Protection**
  * **Article 250 – Grounding and Bonding** : One of the most detailed and critical safety areas in NEC


### **Chapter 3: Wiring Methods and Materials**
  * **Article 300 – General Requirements**
  * **Articles 310–398:**
    * Types of wiring (NM, EMT, MC, etc.)
    * Conduit installation
    * Raceway fill calculations
    * Boxes and fittings


### **Chapter 4: Equipment for General Use**
  * **Article 400 – Flexible Cords and Cables**
  * **Article 408 – Panelboards and Switchboards**
  * **Article 410 – Lighting Systems**
  * **Article 422 – Appliances**
  * **Article 430 – Motors and Motor Circuits**


### **Chapter 6: Special Equipment**
  * **Article 690 – Solar Photovoltaic (PV) Systems**
  * **Article 625 – Electric Vehicle Charging**
  * **Article 706 – Energy Storage Systems (ESS)**
  * **Article 702 – Optional Standby Systems (generators)**
  * Addresses **microgrids, battery backup, and hybrid installations** , which are growing in popularity in Mississippi.


### **Chapter 5: Special Occupancies**
  * Includes detailed rules for:
    * **Health care facilities**
    * **Marinas and boatyards**
    * **Agricultural buildings**
    * **Mobile homes and RVs**
    * **Hazardous locations (Class I, II, III)**


### **Chapter 7: Special Conditions**
  * **Wiring in damp and corrosive environments**
  * **Requirements for temporary power**
  * **Fire alarm and signaling systems**


## **Mississippi-Specific Practices and Modifications**
Area | Description  
---|---  
**Wind & Surge Protection** | Stronger surge protection may be required in coastal counties (especially for service entrances and HVAC disconnects).  
**Flood Zones** | Panelboards and receptacles must be installed above base flood elevation where FEMA maps apply.  
**Agricultural Sites** | Local jurisdictions may apply stricter wiring methods in barns, grain storage, and poultry facilities.  
**Underground Installations** | Depth and conduit requirements vary slightly by soil conditions; enforced at the local level.  
**Inspections** | The State Fire Marshal or local AHJ conducts inspections for most commercial and rural installations.  
## Permit & Inspection Requirements
Electrical permits are required for:
  * New residential and commercial installations
  * Service upgrades (e.g., 100A to 200A)
  * Generator and solar system installations
  * Electrical panel relocation
  * Any commercial-level rewiring


**Inspections typically include:**
  1. Rough-in inspection (before insulation/drywall)
  2. Service inspection (meter base and disconnect)
  3. Final inspection (upon completion of installation)


## Key Safety Features in NEC 2023
  * **Expanded GFCI coverage** : Now includes additional outlets in basements, laundry areas, and outdoors
  * **Updated surge protection** : Required for all dwelling unit services
  * **New requirements for load management** : Helps accommodate EVs and solar battery storage
  * **QR-coded labeling and digital submittals** : Supported for panel schedules and permit documentation


## Referenced Standards
The Mississippi Electrical Code enforces the following standards through NEC:
  * **NFPA 70 (NEC 2023)**
  * **NFPA 70E** – Electrical safety in the workplace
  * **UL Listings** – For electrical equipment and appliances
  * **IEEE/ANSI** – For testing and grounding standards
  * **NEMA** – Enclosure ratings (NEMA 1, 3R, 4X, etc.)


## Summary Table
Section | Coverage  
---|---  
Article 100 | Definitions  
Articles 200–250 | Grounding, overcurrent protection, branch circuits  
Articles 300–398 | Wiring methods and conductors  
Articles 400–490 | Equipment, motors, appliances  
Article 625 | EV charging systems  
Article 690 | Solar systems  
Article 706 | Battery energy storage systems  
Article 210.8 | GFCI/AFCI protection  
The **Mississippi Electrical Code 2023** , based on NEC 2023, represents one of the most **comprehensive and modern sets of electrical safety requirements in the U.S.** It is designed to evolve with technology—covering everything from traditional lighting to solar, batteries, and electric vehicle infrastructure.
!mg author box
Maureen Guttman
AIA  
President  
Washington, DC

# Policy Action Toolkit
Building codes establish the minimum standards of acceptable building practice, so _energy codes_ define the least efficient buildings that can be constructed. Concerns about energy ranging from cost to carbon emissions have led states and municipalities to identify energy codes as an cost-effective policy to use in achieving various goals. Codes can become a viable piece of a broad state plan when milestones for improved efficiency are established.
States and local jurisdictions that are interested in adopting new (or updating existing) code legislation can benefit by reviewing actions others have taken. Samples of existing laws from states and cities around the country can be found on the [Policy Action for Energy Code Reform Page](https://www.bcapcodes.org/policy-action-toolkit/energy-code-reform/).
While the pieces of legislation featured in this section are among the best practices in the country, they may need to be adjusted for your particular state or jurisdiction. Many can be strengthened or tailored to fit the needs of your area. If your jurisdiction writes a bill that could be used as a model by others, please let us know!
  * ##### Connecting with Stakeholders
Each state approaches the energy code adoption process differently. In most states, codes are adopted through the state congress and pass through both the house and senate sides. No matter who is making the decisions on energy codes, making your voice heard is invaluable to the adoption process.
  * ##### [Energy Code Reform](https://www.bcapcodes.org/policy-action-toolkit/energy-code-reform/)
Setting state-level expectations for improving efficiency can provide a common goal for a state’s government and code community to work toward and can ensure support for codes within state government as a valuable part of high-level strategy. We have categorized energy code policy actions into four different levels.
  * ##### Energy Codes and ARRA
The American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009 (Recovery Act) provided two opportunities for states to receive stimulus funds linked to building energy codes: Energy Efficiency and Conservation Block Grants and State Energy Programs (SEPs).


  * ##### Making the Case for Building Energy Codes
Find sample support letters, sample press releases, outreach materials, and consumer resources here.
  * ##### Public Buildings Policy
This page depicts state-level policies for public buildings across the United States.
  * ##### Understanding the Legislative Process
How do states adopt energy codes? Most use either a regulatory process, a legislative process, or a combination of the two. However, some states are home rule, adopting and enforcing their codes at the local level.
  * ##### Where to Begin: Types of Energy Codes
Selecting the most current national model energy code (the 2015 IECC or ASHRAE 90.1-2013) ensures that code reflects changes in technology and design that offer increased energy efficiency.


  * Important links

# Local Implementation Action Kit
##### My state has adopted a new energy code. **_Now what?_**
A new or updated energy code can be a large undertaking, and preparations must be made in order to ensure that the code is properly implemented at the local jurisdiction level. This Local Implementation Action Kit will provide you with the guidance necessary to pass an energy code through the necessary governing body, update the building department with the necessary resources to enforce the code, and evaluate compliance.
##### Step 1: Energy Codes as Good Policy
Buildings impact our society in a big way: **they account for 40-50% of energy use in the United States** and cost consumers thousands of dollars every year. In fact, the average homeowner spends **over $2,000 on utility bills annually**. Energy codes, which set the standards for energy efficient construction , is one of the cheapest, cleanest, and easiest ways to push the market toward a sustainable future while consumers reap the enormous financial benefits.
Now that your state has adopted the energy code as mandatory policy, your municipality should begin to take the necessary measures to implement it at the local level. This will vary from state to state and may be through a legislative process, a regulatory process, or your jurisdiction may be expected to automatically begin enforcing the state law. For support and sample local ordinances, see our Policy Action Toolkit.
* * *
##### Step 2: Changes to the Current Energy Code
The changes from one energy code to another will vary from state to state. However, there are many general similarities that can be found across code updates. In order to understand which changes are applicable to construction in your municipality, you must first determine your climate zone. Depending on your climate zone, you can expect a number of building elements from the list below to be added or improved.
**What is covered by the energy code?** Here is a short list:
  * Standards for insulation in the walls, basement, attic, and floors
  * Energy efficient windows
  * Standards for ventilation and air tightness throughout a building
  * Requirements to seal and/or insulate ductwork
  * Requirement to install a programmable thermostat
  * Requirements for energy efficient lighting


* * *
##### Step 3: Preparing the Building Department Staff
To ensure that your building department properly enforces the updated energy code, plans examiners and site inspectors need to be kept up-to-date on all of the new requirements. A good way to begin is to conduct a training assessment, which enables an internal or external party to observe current practices and make recommendations for improving compliance through education.
Once the building department has a good sense of its training needs, it should make preparations to get its staff up to date on the new energy code requirements. If bringing in a trainer to the department is too expensive, there are other low- and no-cost options. One popular option is to send code officials to a training sponsored by a Regional ICC Chapter, which offer training to run parallel with recent code adoptions. Opportunities also exist to collaborate with other nearby building departments to leverage funding.
Additionally, it is imperative that the building department be on the same page with local-level leadership as to how to report compliance to the state. This is often covered to a minor extent as a part of energy code training, but a meeting may be necessary to cover all of the details and ensure that the state receives all of the necessary data.
##### Additional Training Resources Available:
  * [Energy Code Ambassadors Program](https://www.bcapcodes.org/projects/ecap/)
  * [Compliance Evaluation Checklists](https://www.aceee.org/files/proceedings/2014/data/papers/4-352.pdf)
  * Energy Codes Training Catalog
  * ICC Education, Certification, and Testing
  * [Resource Guides](https://www.energycodes.gov/sites/default/files/2019-09/BECP_Resource_Guide_Policy_Makers.pdf)


##### Step 4: Compliance Evaluation
To make sure that all of the implementation efforts by your jurisdiction, and the state as a whole, are working, compliance evaluation becomes an imperative task. By collecting data on the success of energy code compliance, it gives governing bodies the ability to determine what is working, and which efforts can be improved. In addition, compliance evaluation becomes necessary as the state demonstrates compliance with the 2009 IECC as per Recovery Act requirements.
Although it is up to each state energy office to decide how to evaluate compliance at the state level, the U.S. Department of Energy Building Energy Codes Program (BECP) has published various guidelines and resources designed to assist states and jurisdictions with this process. In many cases, these resources can be used as a starting block, as opposed to starting from scratch, when determining how compliance shall be measured and reported. One useful tool is [Score+Store](https://energycode.pnl.gov/ScoreStore/login), a completely electronic database where building departments can enter energy inspection data and store it into a state profile, which will determine a n overall compliance rate. The program is based off of standard energy inspection checklists, which reduces the amount of extra work and clutter that would otherwise come with reporting data.
In addition to reporting to the state and to DOE, the building department should also use this data internally to determine which areas of the energy code, if any, are trouble spots and increase education on those particular code elements. This cycle of evaluation and improvement is crucial to ensuring long-term compliance with the energy code.
[View BECP’s archived compliance evaluation resources here](https://www.energycodes.gov/sites/default/files/2019-09/ACE_Compliance_Toolkit.pdf).
* * *
##### Step 5: Building Community Outreach
The building community, which typically includes builders, subcontractors, architects, engineers, and developers, needs to be informed well in advance of an updated energy code. As the stakeholders who will be governed by these new codes, the building community should be given plenty of time to prepare itself, attend training, and collect compliance resources.
One of the best ways to reach out to these stakeholders is through local chapters of their professional groups, including the National Association of Home Builders (NAHB) and American Institute of Architects (AIA). These groups represent a large contingency of professional energy code stakeholders who have good reason to disseminate information on the changes that will affect how they do their jobs. It is also imperative that the local building department post the new code and a summary of changes to its own website prior to code enforcement. Some building departments hold a meeting open to the public to explain updates to the code and how they will enforce it, as well as answer questions.
While it is not technically the responsibility of the jurisdiction and its building department to prepare industry professionals for the new code, ensuring that they have the necessary information and updated support resources will help make the compliance process more efficient. Even building departments with limited resources can utilize low- and no-cost options and outreach strategies, and through consistent information-sharing efforts the building professionals can be well versed in the new code.
##### Outreach Resources Available:
  * NAHB Chapter Directory
  * [AIA Chapter Directory](https://www.aias.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/08/COP-Orientation-Guide.pdf)
  * ASHRAE Chapter Directory
  * [BECP Resource Guides](https://www.energycodes.gov/sites/default/files/2019-09/BECP_Resource_Guide_Policy_Makers.pdf)
  * BCAP Code Builder


##### Step 6: Consumer Outreach
Consumers have an important stake in the affordability of their homes and businesses. However, few know anything about energy codes and assume that the buildings they live and work in already meet recognized standards. Enlightening consumers about the benefits of energy codes and how to determine if their home or office meets the code will ultimately improve compliance, as they will demand it from their builder or building owner.
One of the best ways to reach out to consumers is to distribute educational material through the building department and other various partners. BCAP has already created a number of checklists and guides suitable for educating consumers on energy code basics and why they are important, available in the Consumer Portal. If resources are available, you may also consider creating your own customized messages including the costs of noncompliance to consumers and how to check for compliance with a builder or the building department.
Getting the word out to consumers does not have to mean expensive advertising. As an alternative, your municipality can partner with electric and gas utility providers, environmental advocates, and other stakeholders to promote energy codes. Cross-linking to educational materials or an editorial on energy codes will also help spread the word to members of your community.
  * Important links
  * About Us
  * Help
  * Contact
  * Privacy Policy

# Commercial Code Builder
##### 
  1. HVAC
  2. Air sealing
  3. Fenestration
  4. Insulation
  5. Water heating
  6. Ducts
  7. Ventilation
  8. Lighting


#### HVAC
* * *
![](https://www.bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/08/hvac_0.jpg)Commercial HVAC systems are critical in the design of commercial buildings. Properly designed HVAC systems not only help in providing optimal temperature and humidity comfort to individuals in the building, but also help bring “fresh air” from from outside environments increasing building comfort and health.  
(Read more)
#### Air Sealing
* * *
![](https://www.bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/08/airsealing.jpg)Sealing the building envelope is as equally important in a commercial building as in a residential one. Due to the sheer size of many commercial buildings improperly sealed buildings can result in huge amounts of energy waste.  
(Read more)
#### Fenestration
* * *
![](https://www.bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/08/fenestration.jpg)Many options exist when looking to purchase or install windows, doors, and skylights in a commercial building. An increased number of windows and skylights results in increased daylight inside the building. This also results in the possibility of decreased energy efficiency if the incorrect window is chosen or installed improperly. It is important that fenestration components be chosen and installed for maximum efficiency in commercial buildings.  
(Read more)
#### Insulation
* * *
![](https://www.bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/08/insulation.jpg)Commercial insulation provides a number of additional benefits to commercial buildings. Insulation in commercial buildings not only increases occupant comfort but also occupant safety. Adding adequate insulation to a building prevents condensation, acting as a vapor barrier, and controls noise between offices, or private commercial spaces. Proper installation of commercial insulation is critical in maintaining an energy efficient and comfortable work space.  
(Read more)
#### Water heating
* * *
![](https://bcapcodes.org/commercial-2/)Selecting the appropriate way to heat water in a commercial building is a difficult design, cost, and efficiency decision that owners/operators of commercial establishments must make. Water heating depends on a variety of decisions based on the design and occupancy of a building.  
(Read more)
#### Ducts
* * *
![](https://bcapcodes.org/commercial-2/)Commercial distribution systems are important for a variety of reasons. Most importantly, they circulate air around a building and ensure the comfort and safety of the buildings occupants. Improperly installed distribution systems can lead to huge amounts of energy waste and a increased loss of productivity for building tenants because they can become a major source of indoor air pollution.  
(Read more)
#### Ventilation
* * *
Commercial ventilation systems are essential to the health and safety of a buildings occupants. If components are poorly manufactured or installed improperly not only is the building uncomfortable, but the risk of poor indoor air quality is raised substantially.  
(Read more)
#### Lighting
* * *
![](https://bcapcodes.org/commercial-2/)Lighting is a critical component of energy use today, especially in large office buildings. In a large commercial building high performance lighting not only increases comfort but can be a key component of an energy efficient building. A number of lighting systems have been developed to increase occupant comfort and dramatically reduce energy consumption in our commercial buildings.  
(Read more)
#### Commercial Code Status
* * *

# Mississippi Building Code 2024
**Adopts Without Amendments: International Building Code 2024 (IBC 2024)**  
**Effective Statewide: January 1, 2024**
Discover more
Insulation
Building insulation
The Mississippi Building Code 2024 is based on the IBC 2024 and applies to all commercial construction throughout the state. This code ensures that all structures meet essential safety, durability, and energy performance standards suited to Mississippi’s diverse environment—from tornado-prone inland zones to hurricane-vulnerable coastal regions.
## **Chapter 1 – Scope and Administration**
Chapter 1 of the Mississippi Building Code 2024 establishes the **legal framework** for how the entire building code is applied, interpreted, and enforced within the state. It’s essentially the backbone of the code and ensures **uniform enforcement** , **authority boundaries** , and **due process** for all stakeholders — from building owners and contractors to code officials.
This chapter is divided into two main parts:
  * **Part I – Scope and Application (Sections 101–102)**
  * **Part II – Administration and Enforcement (Sections 103–116)**


## **Part I – Scope and Application**
### **Section 101 – Title, Scope, and Intent**
  * **101.1 Title:** This set of regulations is officially titled the **Mississippi Building Code 2024** , based on IBC 2024.
  * **101.2 Scope:** Applies to **construction** , **alteration** , **relocation** , **replacement** , **repair** , **equipment installation** , and **demolition** of buildings and structures, excluding those under the scope of the IRC (residential code) unless specifically referenced.
  * **101.3 Intent:** The intent is to **safeguard public health, safety, and general welfare** , including:
    * Structural strength
    * Means of egress
    * Fire protection
    * Energy conservation
    * Accessibility


### **Section 102 – Applicability and Reference to Other Codes**
  * Outlines how the building code works alongside other adopted codes such as:
    * **Mississippi Electrical Code (NEC 2023)**
    * **Plumbing Code (IPC 2021)**
    * **Mechanical Code (IMC 2021)**
    * **Fire Code (IFC 2021)**
  * **Conflicts of code:** When two code provisions conflict, the more restrictive applies.
  * **Referenced standards (Section 102.4):** Includes standards like NFPA, ASCE, ACI, ANSI, and more. These references are enforceable as part of the code.


## **Part II – Administration and Enforcement**
### **Section 103 – Department of Building Safety**
  * Establishes the **authority and structure** of the department or office responsible for code enforcement.
  * Includes powers of the **Building Official** , who is designated by the jurisdiction to administer and interpret the code.


### **Section 104 – Duties and Powers of the Building Official**
  * The official can:
    * Approve or reject construction documents
    * Conduct inspections
    * Issue stop-work orders
    * Grant modifications if the strict application of code is impractical
  * This section includes **accountability measures** and encourages **due process** for decision-making.


### **Section 105 – Permits**
  * Defines when a building permit is required and what activities are exempt (e.g., minor repairs or cosmetic work).
  * Lists documents and fees required for submission and approval.
  * **Permit validity and expiration:** Work must start within a defined timeframe and be completed before the permit expires.


### **Section 106 – Floor and Site Plans Submission**
  * Requires scaled plans showing:
    * Lot lines and setbacks
    * Proposed structure location
    * Fire access and hydrant locations
    * Construction details


### **Section 107 – Submittal Documents**
  * Specifies minimum requirements for architectural/engineering drawings.
  * Includes digital submission allowances (QR codes, cloud docs, etc.)
  * **Sealed plans** may be required for certain building types.


### **Section 108 – Fees**
  * Allows jurisdictions to set and collect fees for:
    * Permit applications
    * Plan reviews
    * Re-inspections
    * Temporary certificate of occupancy


### **Section 109 – Inspections**
  * Lists mandatory inspection stages (foundation, framing, MEP rough-in, final, etc.)
  * Describes right-of-entry rules and record-keeping protocols.


### **Section 110 – Certificate of Occupancy**
  * Required for:
    * New construction
    * Change of occupancy
    * Significant alterations
  * No building may be legally occupied without one.


### **Section 111 – Temporary Occupancy**
  * Allows temporary use of buildings under certain safety conditions before full code compliance is met.


### **Section 112 – Service Utilities**
  * Building officials have the authority to approve, disconnect, or condemn utilities (gas, electric, water) during unsafe conditions.


### **Section 113 – Appeals**
  * Property owners and professionals can appeal the building official’s decisions through a local **Board of Appeals**.
  * Ensures fair, lawful dispute resolution.


### **Section 114 – Violations**
  * Outlines penalties for noncompliance, including stop-work orders and potential legal action.


### **Section 115 – Unsafe Structures and Equipment**
  * Mandates action when a building poses imminent danger due to collapse, fire risk, or unfit habitation.


### **Section 116 – Emergency Measures**
  * Gives officials authority to act immediately to safeguard public safety in an emergency, even bypassing permit procedures if necessary.


## **Key Highlights of Chapter 1**
Element | Summary  
---|---  
Enforcement Authority | Jurisdiction defines the “building official” with broad enforcement power  
Due Process | Includes right to appeal, public hearings, and administrative review  
Permit Framework | Clear rules for submission, inspection, renewal, and revocation  
Referenced Standards | External standards like NFPA, ASCE, and ACI are enforceable  
Digital & QR Code Support | New provisions support e-submission and QR-coded revisions  
* * *
## Mississippi-Specific Notes
  * Local jurisdictions may adopt Chapter 1 **with minor modifications** , but most follow the ICC standard format.
  * A few counties (especially along the coast) may enforce stricter administrative rules for flood zones and hurricane resilience.
  * Mississippi’s enforcement of Chapter 1 is legally backed by **Mississippi Code §17-2-1 through §17-2-11**.


## **Chapter 2 – Definitions**
**Mississippi Building Code 2024 (Based on IBC 2024)**
### **Overview**
Chapter 2 provides the **official definitions** for terms used throughout the Mississippi Building Code. These definitions are legally binding and ensure that code requirements are interpreted **consistently** by architects, engineers, contractors, inspectors, and building officials.
This chapter is **critical** for establishing clarity. It avoids confusion and ensures that everyone involved in the design, construction, and enforcement process is on the same page.
### **How It Works**
  * Definitions in Chapter 2 apply **wherever the term appears** in the rest of the building code.
  * If a word is **not defined in Chapter 2** , its meaning should be taken from **common usage** , as interpreted by building officials.
  * Some definitions refer to **external standards** (like ASCE, ACI, NFPA, etc.) that are incorporated by reference.


## **Key Categories of Defined Terms**
Here’s a breakdown of the main types of terms defined in this chapter:
### **Occupancy and Use Classifications**
Terms used to assign buildings into use groups (based on Chapter 3). Examples include:
  * **Assembly Group A:** Places used for gathering people, like theaters and churches
  * **Business Group B:** Office spaces and professional services
  * **Residential Group R:** Dwellings, apartments, hotels
  * **High-Hazard Group H:** Buildings storing or using hazardous materials


These definitions are vital because they **affect fire ratings, egress, allowable height/area** , and other code decisions.
### 2. **Construction Types and Fire Resistance**
Terms like:
  * **Fire Wall, Fire Barrier, Smoke Partition, Shaft Enclosure**
  * **Type I, II, III, IV, V Construction**


These help determine what structural systems and materials are allowed based on fire-resistance needs.
### 3. **Egress and Accessibility**
Egress refers to the path people take to safely exit a building in an emergency. Key terms include:
  * **Exit Access** , **Exit Discharge** , **Means of Egress**
  * **Accessible Route** , **Accessible Means of Egress** , **Clear Width**


These definitions link directly to design in Chapter 10 (Means of Egress) and Chapter 11 (Accessibility).
### 4. **Energy and Environmental Performance**
Terms relevant to energy efficiency (Chapter 13) and environmental quality (Chapter 12), such as:
  * **Building Thermal Envelope**
  * **Continuous Insulation (CI)**
  * **SHGC (Solar Heat Gain Coefficient)**
  * **U-factor**


These are especially important for compliance with IECC 2021 as adopted in Mississippi.
### 5. **Structural and Load Design**
Terms referenced in Chapter 16 and related to ASCE 7-22, such as:
  * **Dead Load** , **Live Load** , **Wind Load** , **Risk Category**
  * **Flood Hazard Area** , **Special Flood Hazard Area (SFHA)**
  * **Importance Factor** (used for hospitals, emergency centers)


These terms define how buildings resist natural forces like wind, flood, or seismic activity.
### 6. **Systems and Equipment**
Used in mechanical, electrical, and plumbing (MEP) design:
  * **Plenum** , **Duct** , **Air-Handling Unit** , **Backflow Preventer**
  * **Fire Dampers** , **Combustion Air** , **Grease Ducts**


These definitions support Chapters 27–29 and the referenced mechanical/plumbing codes (IMC/IPC).
### 7. **Specialized Uses or Features**
For unique building elements or advanced technologies:
  * **Green Roof** , **Mass Timber** , **Photovoltaic Panel System**
  * **Storm Shelter** , **Atrium** , **Open Parking Garage**


These help enforce specialized code chapters (e.g., Chapter 4, 15, or 31).
##  !✅ **Important Notes for Chapter 2**
Topic | Summary  
---|---  
**Scope** | Applies to all chapters unless a more specific definition is provided  
**Legal Authority** | Definitions are enforceable in court, inspections, and permits  
**External References** | Links to standards like NFPA, ASCE, and ICC A117.1  
**State Amendments** | Mississippi currently uses the unamended IBC 2024 version, so definitions match ICC’s model code  
**Example Definitions from Chapter 2**
Term | Definition  
---|---  
**Means of Egress** | A continuous and unobstructed path of vertical and horizontal travel from any occupied portion of a building to a public way  
**Fire Area** | The aggregate floor area enclosed by fire walls, fire barriers, or exterior walls  
**Accessory Occupancy** | A space secondary to the main occupancy that does not exceed 10% of the building area  
**Atrium** | A large vertical open space within a building that connects two or more stories  
**Special Flood Hazard Area** | An area with special flood risks as defined by FEMA and local floodplain ordinances  
## Why This Chapter Matters
  * **Clarifies Intent** – Reduces legal ambiguity in code enforcement
  * **Prevents Misinterpretation** – Ensures consistent application across all parties
  * **Supports Enforcement** – Code officials can refer to exact wording when issuing corrections
  * **Links Chapters Together** – Definitions are the glue between occupancy, construction type, egress, and systems


!mg author box
Maureen Guttman
AIA  
President  
Washington, DC

# Ohio Building Codes
#### Current Commercial Code
!ohio building codes
This code is mandatory statewide for the construction, alteration, movement, enlargement, replacement, repair, equipment, use and occupancy, location, maintenance, removal and demolition of buildings under its purview. The OBC is generally applicable to construction that is not detached one-, two-, or three-family dwellings.
**2024 Ohio Building Code**
**Adoption Info**
Discover more
Lumber
insulation
Timber
Official title  
2024 Ohio Building Code (OBC)
Overview  
The 2024 Ohio Building Code is based on the 2021 International Building Code (IBC) with Ohio-specific amendments. It sets the minimum requirements for building design, construction, and safety in commercial buildings within the state of Ohio. This code ensures that commercial structures are built to provide safety, public health, and general welfare through structural strength, means of egress, stability, sanitation, light and ventilation, energy conservation, and safety to life and property from fire and other hazards attributed to the built environment.

#### Current Residential Code
This code is mandatory statewide for the construction, alteration, movement, enlargement, replacement, repair, equipment, use and occupancy, location, maintenance, removal, and demolition of every one-, two-, or three-family dwelling.
**Climate Zones:** 4A, 5A
* * *
#### Code Adoption and Change Process
  1. **Proposal Submission** : 
     * Stakeholders, including architects, engineers, builders, trade associations, government officials, and the public, can propose changes to the building codes. Proposals are typically submitted during a designated window period.
  2. **Initial Review** : 
     * The OBBS staff reviews the proposals for completeness and relevance. Proposals that meet the initial criteria are forwarded to the appropriate committee for further consideration.
  3. **Committee Review** : 
     * Technical Advisory Committees (TACs), composed of experts in specific areas like plumbing, electrical, or mechanical systems, review the proposals. These committees evaluate the technical merits and implications of the proposals.
     * The committees may hold public meetings where proponents and other interested parties can discuss and provide additional information about the proposed changes.
  4. **Public Comment and Hearings** : 
     * After the committee review, the OBBS publishes the proposed changes and opens them for public comment. This period allows the public and industry stakeholders to submit written comments or express concerns.
     * Public hearings may be scheduled to gather more extensive feedback and discuss the proposed changes in a public forum.
  5. **Recommendations and Modifications** : 
     * Based on the feedback and discussions during the public comment period and hearings, the TACs may revise the proposals. They then submit their recommendations to the full Board of Building Standards.
  6. **Board Review and Decision** : 
     * The full Board of Building Standards reviews the committee recommendations and public inputs. The board then votes on whether to adopt the proposed changes.
     * If approved, the changes are finalized and integrated into the Ohio Building Code.
  7. **Filing and Publication** : 
     * The approved changes are filed with the Secretary of State and published. There is typically a lead time between the publication and the effective date of the new regulations, allowing the industry to adapt to the new standards.
  8. **Implementation** : 
     * The new or revised codes become effective on the specified date. The OBBS often provides training sessions and resources to help stakeholders understand and comply with the new regulations.


##### Code Change Cycle
### 2022: Assessment and Initial Review
  * **Activities** : Review and analysis of the 2021 ICC model codes.
  * **Stakeholder Engagement** : Initial feedback sessions with industry professionals, public agencies, and other stakeholders.


### 2023: Development and Proposal Submission
  * **Proposal Submission** : Open call for proposals based on the new model codes and specific needs identified within Oregon.
  * **Review by Committees** : Technical committees begin evaluating proposals for technical merit and applicability.


##### Next Code Update
### 2024: Public Review and Final Decisions
  * **Public Comment** : Proposed changes are published for public review and comment.
  * **Public Hearings** : Hearings are held to discuss detailed implications of proposals and gather further input.
  * **Finalization of Changes** : Regulatory bodies review committee recommendations and public input, then make final decisions on code adoption.


### 2025: Adoption and Implementation
  * **Adoption** : Approved changes are formally adopted and published.
  * **Effective Date** : New or revised codes become effective, typically at the beginning of the year.
  * **Education and Transition** : Training and educational programs are offered to help implement the new codes effectively.


### 2026 and Beyond: Monitoring and Preparations for Next Cycle
  * **Ongoing Evaluation** : Continuous monitoring of the implementation of the new codes to identify any issues or further amendments.
  * **Early Preparations** : Start preparations for the next cycle based on the upcoming ICC code updates expected in 2024.


##### Next Code Update
* * *
#### History
**January 21, 2011** The Ohio BBS approves a set of amendments to update the state’s construction codes. Part D would update the Ohio Building Code (OBC) to incorporate the 2009 International Building Code (IBC), including its references to the 2009 IECC and ASHRAE 90.1-2007 as compliance paths for energy efficiency for non-residential buildings. The OBC currently references the 2006 IBC.
**July 1, 1979** | Rule Adoption | The Ohio Board of Building Standards repeals most of the existing Ohio Building Code, adopting a collection of model code sections and superseding Ohio provisions, including the CABO Model Energy Code (MEC).  
---|---|---  
**March 1, 1998** | MEC Adoption | The 1995 MEC is adopted and becomes effective statewide.  
**July 1, 1995** | Energy Code Implementation | The 1993 MEC and ASHRAE 90.1-1989 go into effect, enhancing energy conservation measures.  
**March 1, 2005** | Energy Code Update | The 2003 IECC is adopted and becomes effective.  
**January 1, 2008** | 2008 Ohio Building Code Implementation | The 2008 Ohio Building Code (OBC) based on the 2006 IBC goes into effect, referencing the 2006 IECC and ASHRAE 90.1-2004 as compliance paths.  
**January 1, 2009** | Adoption of 2006 IECC | BBS adopts the 2006 IECC for the second time with additional compliance options for residential buildings.  
**March 7, 2011** | JCARR Approval | JCARR approves updates to Ohio’s nonresidential energy standards, effective November 1, 2011.  
**May 11, 2011** | Alternative Energy Code Approval | The Residential Codes Advisory Committee approves the OHBA Residential Alternative Energy Code for the next edition of the ORC.  
**November 1, 2011** | Code Updates | Updates to the OBC, Ohio Mechanical Code, and Ohio Plumbing Code become effective.  
**May 28, 2012** | RCO Update | Updates the RCO to reference the 2009 IECC with state-developed alternative compliance paths, raising insulation requirements and energy efficiency standards.  
**January 1, 2013** | 2013 RCO Implementation | The 2013 Residential Code of Ohio becomes effective, governing new and renovated residential construction.  
**September 30, 2016** | Amendments Group 92 Adoption | The Ohio Board of Building Standards adopts rule changes to OBC Chapter 13, focusing on energy efficiency, effective January 1, 2017.

# Mississippi Fire Code 2024
**Base Code:** 2021 International Fire Code (IFC 2021)
Discover more
extinguishers
Fire extinguishers
fire alarm
**Effective Date:** July 1, 2024
**Adopting Authority:** Mississippi State Fire Marshal’s Office
**Applies To:** All new and existing commercial buildings, residential care facilities, high-occupancy buildings, educational occupancies, industrial facilities, and other high-risk uses across the state
The **Mississippi Fire Code 2024** is based on the **2021 International Fire Code (IFC)** and establishes statewide minimum fire safety standards. It is designed to protect **life, property, and the environment** from fire and explosion hazards by regulating:
  * Fire prevention systems,
  * Fire service access,
  * Building and system maintenance,
  * Hazardous materials storage and handling,
  * Emergency response protocols.


The code applies to both **new construction** and **existing buildings** (including retroactive provisions for high-risk occupancies).
## **Code Organization by Chapters**
The Fire Code is divided into over 80 chapters and sections. Below is a detailed look at the most critical chapters enforced in Mississippi:
### **Chapter 1 – Scope and Administration**
  * Establishes the authority of the **Fire Code Official** , including inspections, enforcement, stop work orders, and legal action.
  * Describes permitting requirements for high-risk operations (e.g., welding, hazardous storage).
  * Includes appeals processes and penalties for violations.


### **Chapter 3 – General Precautions Against Fire**
  * Fire safety rules for all occupancies, including:
    * Maintenance of fire doors and fire lanes
    * Open flame regulations
    * Proper use of extension cords and temporary wiring
    * Portable heating appliance rules
  * Requirements for vacant buildings, combustible waste, and fireworks handling.


### **Chapter 4 – Emergency Planning and Preparedness**
  * Requires fire evacuation plans and drills for:
    * Group E (Educational)
    * Group I (Institutional)
    * Assembly occupancies (over 50 people)
  * Fire safety plans must be submitted to local authorities for approval in large venues, hospitals, and assisted living facilities.


### **Chapter 5 – Fire Service Features**
  * Minimum standards for:
    * Fire department vehicle access roads
    * Fire hydrant locations and spacing
    * Emergency responder radio coverage
    * Building numbering and identification


### **Chapter 6 – Building Services and Systems**
  * Regulates:
    * Fire alarm systems
    * Standpipe systems
    * Fire extinguishers
    * Emergency power for fire protection systems
    * Smoke control systems in atriums and high-rise buildings


### **Chapter 9 – Fire Protection Systems**
  * Requirements for:
    * **Automatic sprinkler systems** (NFPA 13, 13R, 13D)
    * **Fire alarm systems** (NFPA 72)
    * Fire pumps, water supplies, flow tests
    * Inspection, testing, and maintenance (ITM) protocols
  * Retrofitting may be required for certain high-occupancy or hazardous uses.


### **Chapter 50–67 – Hazardous Materials**
  * Comprehensive safety requirements for:
    * Flammable liquids and gases
    * Corrosives and oxidizers
    * Explosives and fireworks
    * Combustible dust-producing operations
  * Requires special permits, containment systems, fire barriers, and safety plans.


### **Chapter 33 – Fire Safety During Construction and Demolition**
  * Outlines precautions during construction:
    * Fire watch assignments
    * Temporary standpipes and water supplies
    * Control of combustibles and ignition sources
    * Site security and signage


### **Chapter 10 – Means of Egress (Cross-referenced with IBC)**
  * Maintains fire-safe egress during emergencies:
    * Clear exit access and signage
    * Panic hardware on exit doors
    * Occupant load calculations
    * Illumination and emergency lighting


## **Mississippi-Specific Amendments and Practices**
Area | Description  
---|---  
**Sprinkler Requirements** | Coastal counties and public facilities may require sprinklers in occupancies where state law mandates  
**Open Burning** | Controlled and restricted by county with Fire Marshal oversight  
**Fireworks** | Sales, storage, and use regulated under IFC + state-specific firework licensing laws  
**Rural Access Requirements** | Additional fire service access requirements in unincorporated areas  
**Special Industrial Uses** | Inspections required for refineries, chemical plants, and processing facilities storing hazardous materials  
## Referenced Standards
The Mississippi Fire Code references the following national standards:
  * **NFPA 13 / 13R / 13D** – Sprinkler systems
  * **NFPA 72** – Fire alarm systems
  * **NFPA 25** – ITM of water-based fire protection
  * **NFPA 704** – Hazard signage for hazardous materials
  * **ICC A117.1** – Accessibility standards
  * **ASTM, UL, and ANSI** – Equipment and materials


##  Permits and Compliance
The following activities typically require Fire Code permits in Mississippi:
  * Installation of fire alarm/sprinkler systems
  * Storage of flammable liquids or hazardous materials
  * Open flame performances, tents over 400 sq. ft., fireworks
  * Hot work (welding, cutting, torching) operations
  * Change of occupancy in existing buildings


## Enforcement and Inspections
  * Fire Inspectors under the Mississippi State Fire Marshal conduct inspections during:
    * Construction
    * Occupancy approval
    * Periodic routine audits (e.g., hospitals, schools)
  * **Violations** may result in:
    * Stop-work orders
    * Fines
    * Denial of occupancy
    * Legal injunctions for life safety hazards


## Summary Table
Chapter | Focus Area  
---|---  
1 | Scope and enforcement authority  
3 | Fire prevention and general safety  
4 | Emergency planning and evacuation  
5 | Fire service access and utilities  
6 | System controls (alarms, extinguishers, smoke)  
9 | Automatic fire protection systems  
10 | Means of egress  
33 | Fire safety during construction  
50–67 | Hazardous materials safety  
Appendices | Optional but may be adopted locally (fire flow, water supply, etc.)  
## Why the Mississippi Fire Code Matters
  * Helps **protect lives and property** through consistent fire prevention and response regulations
  * Establishes **accountability** for building owners, contractors, and emergency responders
  * Ensures **firefighters have the tools, space, and systems** needed for safe operations
  * Enables **prevention-focused design** in everything from schools to warehouses

Maureen Guttman
AIA
