#### Current Residential Code of Arkansas
The current Residential Code of Arkansas adheres to the 2021 International Residential Code (IRC), which includes comprehensive building, plumbing, mechanical, fuel gas, and electrical requirements for one- and two-family dwellings and townhouses up to three stories. This code is part of a set that also includes volumes on building and fire codes, ensuring a holistic approach to safety and regulatory compliance across different types of constructions.
For more detailed information on the specific provisions of the 2021 Arkansas Fire Prevention Code, Volume III – Residential, or to access the full text of these codes, you can visit the Digital Codes website where they are available:
  * 2021 Arkansas Fire Prevention Code, Volume III – Residential
  * 2021 International Residential Code (IRC)


* * *
#### Code Adoption and Change Process
The current Residential Code of Arkansas adheres to the 2021 International Residential Code (IRC), which encompasses comprehensive regulations for building, plumbing, mechanical, fuel gas, and electrical systems. This code applies to one- and two-family dwellings and townhouses up to three stories high. It is part of a broader set of codes that also includes volumes on building and fire codes, thereby ensuring a comprehensive approach to safety and regulatory compliance across various types of constructions.
For more detailed information on the specific provisions of the 2021 Arkansas Fire Prevention Code, Volume III – Residential, or to access the full text of these codes, they are available online:
  * 2021 Arkansas Fire Prevention Code, Volume III – Residential
  * 2021 International Residential Code (IRC)


These resources provide in-depth coverage of th
##### Code Change Cycle
For the code change cycle in Arkansas, there is no set schedule. Changes to building codes are made as needed, depending on the latest updates to international standards, advancements in building technology, or specific state requirements that arise. This flexible approach allows Arkansas to adapt and respond to new safety and efficiency challenges without being confined to a rigid timeline.
##### Next Code Update
–
_Back to top_
* * *
* * *
#### History
1979 | First Arkansas Energy Code | Enacted based on the 1977 Model Code for Energy Conservation, referencing ASHRAE/IES Standard 90-1975.  
---|---|---  
1993 | Legislative Authority to AEO | Legislation gave the Arkansas Energy Office authority to adopt new standards.  
1994 | New Arkansas Energy Code Adoption | A new code based on the 1992 Model Energy Code for residential buildings and ASHRAE 90.1-1989 for commercial buildings.  
2004 | Update to Arkansas Energy Code | Adopted the 2003 IECC and ASHRAE Standard 90.1-2001, becoming effective on October 1, 2004.  
2009 | Act No. 1494 Enactment | Encouraged conservation of energy in public buildings and set higher efficiency standards.  
2011 | Implementation of Sustainability Rules | Rules for Sustainable Energy Efficient Public Buildings were implemented, requiring public buildings to be at least 10% more efficient than baseline standards.  
2013 | Commercial Code Update | Updated commercial provisions to reference ASHRAE Standard 90.1-2007 and Chapter 5 of the 2009 IECC.  
2014 | Adoption of the 2014 Arkansas Energy Code | Set new minimum energy efficiency standards for new construction, based on the 2009 IECC, with significant amendments.  
2015 | Implementation of 2014 Arkansas Energy Code | The 2014 code became effective, enhancing energy efficiency requirements for new buildings.  
2021 | Update to Arkansas Fire Prevention Code | Volumes I, II, and III updated to align with the 2021 International Codes for comprehensive coverage of safety and compliance.  
2023 | Effective Date of 2021 Arkansas Fire Prevention Code | The updated code volumes became enforceable starting January 1, 2023.  
_Back to top_

# Florida Building Codes
Florida Building Code is a comprehensive set of regulations governing construction and safety standards in the Sunshine State. It covers architectural design, construction materials, and safety measures, ensuring the structural integrity and resilience of buildings throughout Florida.
!florida building code

7th Edition Florida Building Code, based on the 2018 International Building Code (IBC), with Florida-specific amendments. Includes the Florida Building Code, Energy Conservation, based on the 2018 International Energy Conservation Code (IECC), referencing ASHRAE Standard 90.1-2016 with Florida-specific amendments.  
Passed in 2020, effective as of December 31, 2020.
Link to COMcheck
**Current Residential Code**
2020 Florida Building Code – Residential, based on the 2018 International Residential Code (IRC) with Florida-specific amendments. Includes the **2020 Florida Building Code, Energy Conservation** , based on the 2018 International Energy Conservation Code (IECC), referencing ASHRAE Standard 90.1-2016 with Florida-specific amendments.
**Effective as of December 31, 2020.**
#### Link to REScheck
**Climate Zones:** 1A, 2A
* * *
#### Code Adoption and Change Process
##### Code Change Process
The Florida Building Commission (FBC) is directed to adopt, revise, update, and maintain the Florida Building Code in accordance with Chapter 120 of the state statutes. The code is a mandatory uniform statewide code and need not be adopted by a local government to be applicable at the local level. Local jurisdictions may _not_ adopt more or less efficient codes. Visit the Florida Building Commission FAQ page for a detailed overview of the code modification process.
Technical Advisory Committee Review Process (from previous code update).
##### Code Change Cycle
Three year code cycle. Please see this presentation for more information on Florida’s system for updating its codes.
* * *
#### History
**2020** | Adoption of the 7th Edition Florida Building Code, based on the 2018 International Codes (I-Codes) with Florida-specific amendments. Includes significant updates in energy conservation and structural integrity to address Florida’s unique climate and weather challenges. Effective December 31, 2020.  
---|---  
**2017** | Introduction of the 6th Edition Florida Building Code, based on the 2015 I-Codes. Implemented updates focused on energy efficiency through the Florida Building Code, Energy Conservation, referencing the 2015 IECC and ASHRAE Standard 90.1-2013. Effective December 31, 2017.  
**2014** | The 5th Edition Florida Building Code is enacted, adopting and adapting the 2012 I-Codes. This edition included updates to enhance hurricane resistance and energy efficiency, crucial for Florida’s high wind speeds and hot climate. Effective June 30, 2015.  
**2010** | The 4th Edition Florida Building Code comes into effect, integrating the 2009 I-Codes with specific modifications for Florida’s environmental conditions, particularly focusing on wind mitigation techniques for coastal areas. Effective March 15, 2012.  
**2007** | Implementation of the 3rd Edition Florida Building Code, adapting the 2006 I-Codes. This update emphasized strengthening structures against hurricanes and improving fire safety measures. Effective October 1, 2008.  
**2004** | The 2nd Edition of the Florida Building Code is introduced, incorporating the 2003 I-Codes. This edition addressed the lessons learned from recent hurricanes, enhancing requirements for wind load and flood resistance. Effective October 1, 2005.  
**2001** | Florida adopts its first statewide building code, the Florida Building Code, which consolidates various regional codes into a unified state code based on the 1997 Standard Building Code by SBCCI, tailored with Florida-specific modifications to address hurricane impacts effectively. Effective March 1, 2002.  
**1990s** | Prior to the statewide code, Florida relied on local codes that varied significantly across different jurisdictions. The devastating impacts of Hurricane Andrew in 1992 spurred efforts towards a unified state code to address inconsistencies and enhance building safety statewide.

# Compliance Portal
With so much emphasis placed on energy code adoption, it’s easy to overlook implementation and compliance. Yet implementation and compliance with the energy code is the key to achieving the intended energy and cost savings, and for meeting state and national targets for emission reduction and protecting our natural resources. 100 percent compliance is an attainable goal, but requires time, resources, and strategic planning for implementation and enforcement. Below are programs, tools, and resources states and municipalities can use to help set and meet compliance goals.
##### Compliance Planning Assistance
STATE-LEVEL STRATEGIC PLANS
##### Local Implementation Action Kit
GUIDE TO LOCAL CODE ENFORCEMENT
##### IMT Case Studies
BEST PRACTICES FROM THE US AND CHINA
##### Code Builder
GUIDE TO THE MODEL ENERGY CODES
##### Compliance Collaboratives
KEY STAKEHOLDERS PURSUE STATES’ GOALS
##### Energy Code Ambassadors
ESTABLISHING LOCAL EXPERTS
##### State Compliance Studies
MEASURING STATE COMPLIANCE RATES
##### Design Professionals
RESOURCES FOR DESIGN PROFESSIONALS

# Consumer Portal
##### **Buying a new home or remodeling?**
You have a right to an energy efficient home. Energy codes are minimum requirements for the design and construction of new and renovated residential and commercial buildings. They ensure your home meets minimum energy efficiency standards. Energy codes reduce energy demand, save consumers money, improve comfort, and reduce greenhouse gas emissions.
Most states update energy codes every three years in order to keep pace with advancements in technology and construction methods, but some states do not. In those states, building to outdated standards causes home and business owners to pay unnecessarily high energy bill _every month_ for the lifetime of the building.
##### **Why are energy codes important?**
Residential and commercial buildings consume over 40% of energy used in the United States. Modern energy codes protect citizens and our economy from increasingly high energy bills. As a nation, our growing demand for energy impacts:
  * **A family’s standard of living:** Money spent on monthly energy bill could be spent elsewhere.
  * **Our economy:** Every dollar not spent on imported energy is a dollar that remains in our economy. The more energy we import, the more money we export.
  * **The environment:** Energy efficiency reduces air, water, and soil pollution because fewer fossil fuels are burned to meet demand.
  * **U.S. competitiveness:** Lower energy bills make it easier for American businesses to thrive inside its border, and not move overseas.
  * **Jobs:** Increased demand for energy efficient goods and services provides jobs for contractors, insulators, mechanical systems companies, window manufacturers, and more.
  * **Energy rates:** Energy codes help keep rates lower by delaying the need to build costly new power plants, and ease the strain on our nation’s aging utility grid that delivers energy from power plants to our homes and businesses.
  * **National security:** Energy efficiency reduces the amount of energy we need to import from outside the U.S.


##### **Homebuyer Demand for Energy Codes**
Four different consumer surveys reveal that homeowners across America think about energy codes and energy efficiency when buying a new home. You can read the full reports of each of these surveys at the following links:
  * What Home Buyers Really Want
  * Energy Codes Messaging Test: Evaluation by Region
  * [Idaho Homeowner Energy Code Survey](http://www.idahoenergycode.com/sites/idahoenergycode.com/files/resources/pdf/BPA%20Grant%20Idaho%20Energy%20Code%20Survey%2031May2014.pdf)
  * [2015 Public Opinion Survey](http://www.ndare.org/vertical/sites/%7BE50B81F9-F226-4A80-AE75-3CBA3D28BCB4%7D/uploads/ND_Alliance_for_Renewable_Energy_Report_%28REVISED_Mar_5_2015%29.pdf)


#####  **_Take Action!_**  
Spread the word on energy codes to your government official.
Elected officials need to hear from you! They listen to their constituents. Below are some materials to help you:
  * Questions for Your Real Estate Agent and Home Inspector
  * Energy Code Awareness (PowerPoint)
  * Why Energy Codes Matter

Check out the features of a home that meets code.

86% of homeowners want to know a home’s energy operating costs before they buy or rent.  
82% of homeowners believe they have a right to homes that meet national standards.  
77% of homeowners think that homebuilders should not make less efficient homes at the consumer’s expense.

##### About the Consumer Campaign
The Consumer Outreach Campaign was launched by BCAP and Consumers Union in 2011 to educate the public on the impact of home energy use, the role of energy codes in addressing home energy performance, and what you can do to save energy and money.
Our primary goal is to educate and engage everyone in support of energy codes. You have a right to a home that meets national standards for energy efficiency. Read more about the Consumer Campaign.
In June 2015, Consumers Union reaffirmed their support for energy codes by urging the Energy and Commerce Committee to support code compliance efforts.

# Ohio Concrete Construction Tolerance Code 2010
### **Ohio Concrete Construction Tolerance Code 2010 Adoption Info**
**Effective Dates** January 1, 2010 – Present
**Adopts with Amendments** ACI 117-2006 Standard Specifications for Tolerances for Concrete Construction and Materials
Read More: Ohio Building Services Piping Code 2020
Ohio Conveyor and Related Equipment Code 2018
**Overview** ACI 117 is a standard developed by the American Concrete Institute (ACI) that provides guidelines and requirements for the tolerances in concrete construction. It covers the permissible variations in dimensions, position, and other properties of concrete structures and elements to ensure structural integrity and performance.
The 2010 Ohio Concrete Construction Tolerance Code is based on ACI 117-2006 with Ohio-specific amendments. This code sets the minimum standards for the allowable tolerances in concrete construction within the state of Ohio. The Ohio-specific amendments include additional provisions tailored to the unique needs and conditions of concrete construction in Ohio, ensuring quality and consistency in concrete structures.
!authorbox
Cosimina
Cosimina has been a member of BCAP for over a decade, actively contributing to the organization’s nationally acclaimed initiatives aimed at assisting states and local authorities in the establishment and enforcement of robust and efficient building energy codes. Her involvement spans across advocacy, technical guidance, outreach programs, and the formation of strategic coalitions.

# Mississippi Residential Code 2024
## **Mississippi Residential Code 2024**
**Base Code:** 2021 International Residential Code (IRC 2021)  
**Effective Date:** July 1, 2024  
**Scope:** One- and two-family dwellings and townhouses not more than three stories above grade in height  
**Adopting Authority:** Mississippi State Fire Marshal’s Office  
**Applies To:** New construction, additions, alterations, and repairs to detached homes, duplexes, and townhomes
### **Overview**
The **Mississippi Residential Code 2024** governs the **design, construction, and safety** of residential structures throughout the state. Based on the **IRC 2021** , this code integrates structural, mechanical, electrical, plumbing, energy efficiency, and fire safety standards into one comprehensive residential framework.
It is tailored to Mississippi’s hot-humid climate and high-wind zones—especially in Gulf Coast counties. The code includes wind resistance design, flood zone construction requirements, and energy compliance provisions suited for southern conditions.
## **Code Organization by Parts**
The IRC is structured into the following main parts:
### **Part I — Administrative Provisions**
Defines scope, permits, enforcement, and rights of inspection. These mirror the IBC Chapter 1 provisions but are streamlined for residential projects.
### **Part II — Building Planning and Construction**
#### **Chapters 3–10: General Design and Structural Requirements**
  * **Chapter 3 – Building Planning:**
    * Minimum room sizes
    * Ceiling height, stair geometry, emergency escape openings
    * Safety glazing and fire separation between garage/living spaces
  * **Chapter 4 – Foundations:**
    * Footing size, reinforcement, frost protection
    * Foundation walls and anchoring requirements
    * Foundation venting and waterproofing
  * **Chapter 5 – Floors:**
    * Joist spans, headers, decking, cantilever limits
    * Mass timber and engineered lumber allowances
  * **Chapter 6 – Wall Construction:**
    * Wood and steel framing
    * Braced wall panels and shear wall requirements
    * Wall stud sizing tables
  * **Chapter 7 – Wall Covering:**
    * Siding, lath, vapor retarders
    * Water-resistive barriers and flashing
  * **Chapter 8 – Roof-Ceiling Construction:**
    * Rafters, ceiling joists, structural ridge support
    * Wind uplift anchoring
    * Insulation above roof decks
  * **Chapter 9 – Roof Assemblies:**
    * Roof materials and fire classification
    * Ice barriers and drip edges
    * Underlayment and ventilation
  * **Chapter 10 – Chimneys and Fireplaces:**
    * Masonry and factory-built fireplace requirements
    * Clearances to combustibles
    * Chimney heights and spark arrestors


### **Part III — Building Systems**
#### **Chapters 11–23: Energy, Plumbing, Mechanical, and Electrical**
  * **Chapter 11 – Energy Efficiency (Based on IECC 2021):**
    * Building envelope insulation values
    * SHGC/U-factor limits for windows and doors
    * Duct leakage limits and blower door testing
  * **Chapters 12–23 – Mechanical Systems:**
    * HVAC equipment sizing using Manual J
    * Duct design (Manual D) and ventilation standards
    * Fuel gas piping and combustion air (IFGC-based)
  * **Chapters 24–33 – Plumbing Systems (Based on IPC):**
    * Water supply pipe sizing
    * Drainage systems and venting
    * Water heater safety and backflow prevention
  * **Chapters 34–43 – Electrical Systems (Based on NEC 2023):**
    * Outlet spacing, GFCI/AFCI requirements
    * Service entrance sizing
    * Smoke alarms and carbon monoxide detectors


### **Part IV — Specialty Codes**
  * **Chapter 44 – Referenced Standards**
    * Lists external standards such as ASTM, ANSI, NFPA 13D, ASHRAE, ACI, and ICC documents used throughout the IRC.


## **Mississippi-Specific Considerations**
Topic | State-Specific Focus  
---|---  
**Wind Load Design** | Required in southern/coastal counties using ASCE 7 and IRC wind tables  
**Flood Zone Construction** | Buildings in flood-prone areas must comply with FEMA and local floodplain ordinances  
**Energy Efficiency** | Enforced per IECC 2021 with added focus on attic insulation, window shading, and HVAC duct testing  
**Foundation Type** | Raised slab or piers are commonly used in flood zones along the Gulf Coast  
**Sprinkler Systems** | NFPA 13D applies where systems are voluntarily installed or required by local ordinance  
## **Approved Compliance Tools for Residential Code in Mississippi**
  * **Manual J/S/D** for HVAC system design
  * **REScheck** for energy code compliance under IECC 2021
  * **WINDspeed app (FEMA)** for determining local design wind speeds
  * **FEMA Flood Insurance Rate Maps (FIRMs)** for flood hazard zones
  * **AHJ checklists** from local counties for permit submittals


## **Summary Table**
Code Section | Description  
---|---  
Chapters 3–10 | Structural Design and Planning  
Chapter 11 | Energy Conservation (IECC 2021)  
Chapters 12–23 | Mechanical Systems  
Chapters 24–33 | Plumbing Systems  
Chapters 34–43 | Electrical Systems  
Chapter 44 | Referenced Standards  
## **Legal Framework and Enforcement**
  * Enforced by the **local authority having jurisdiction (AHJ)** , under the statewide mandate of the State Fire Marshal
  * Jurisdictions may adopt **stricter requirements** , especially in areas prone to wind or flood risk
  * Permits, inspections, and final occupancy certifications are required before use


## **Chapter 1: Scope and Administration**
**Applies To:** One- and two-family dwellings and townhouses not more than three stories in height  
**Base Code:** IRC 2021, adopted statewide as of July 1, 2024
### **Overview**
Chapter 1 provides the **legal framework and enforcement structure** for the Mississippi Residential Code. It defines:
  * Which buildings are covered,
  * Who has authority to enforce the code,
  * How permits are issued and revoked,
  * What processes are used for inspections, violations, and appeals.


This chapter is **critical** because it ensures **consistency and accountability** across all counties and cities adopting the code.
## **Section-by-Section Breakdown**
### **Section R101 – General**
  * **R101.1 Title:**  
Officially titled the _Mississippi Residential Code 2024_ , based on the _2021 IRC_.
  * **R101.2 Scope:**  
Applies to:
    * Detached one- and two-family dwellings
    * Townhouses not more than three stories
    * Their accessory structures (e.g., garages, decks)
**Excludes** commercial buildings, multifamily apartments, or any building classified under the IBC.
  * **R101.3 Intent:**  
The purpose is to:
    * Ensure public safety and health
    * Provide reasonable energy efficiency
    * Support property protection through standardized design and construction practices


### **Section R102 – Applicability**
  * **R102.1 General:**  
The code applies uniformly unless a local authority modifies it with stronger standards.
  * **R102.2 Other Laws:**  
This code does not override:
    * Zoning laws
    * Environmental laws
    * FEMA floodplain ordinances
    * Historic preservation codes
  * **R102.3 Application of References:**  
Where the code references other standards (e.g., ASTM, NFPA), those standards become enforceable.
  * **R102.4 Referenced Codes:**  
Links to:
    * **Mississippi Mechanical Code** (IMC 2021)
    * **Mississippi Plumbing Code** (IPC 2021)
    * **Mississippi Electrical Code** (NEC 2023)
    * **Energy Code** (IECC 2021)
  * **R102.5 Appendices:**  
Appendices (like A for decks or Q for tiny homes) are **not enforceable** unless **specifically adopted** by the local jurisdiction.


### **Section R103 – Department of Building Safety**
  * Establishes the building inspection department or office within each jurisdiction (city, county).
  * Authorizes the **Building Official** to:
    * Review plans
    * Issue permits
    * Enforce stop-work orders
    * Perform inspections


### **Section R104 – Duties and Powers of the Building Official**
  * **Authority to Interpret Code:**  
The building official can issue interpretations consistent with the code’s intent.
  * **Stop Work Orders:**  
Can halt construction if work is unsafe or non-compliant.
  * **Modifications:**  
May approve alternative materials or construction methods if they meet code intent and performance.
  * **Technical Assistance:**  
Can require engineers/architects to provide reports if the project exceeds typical structural or environmental design limits.


### **Section R105 – Permits**
  * **When Required:**  
Permits are required for:
    * New buildings
    * Additions
    * Structural alterations
    * Electrical, plumbing, HVAC system work
  * **When Not Required (Exempt Work):**
    * Cosmetic repairs (paint, wallpaper)
    * Non-structural fences
    * Prefabricated sheds <200 sq. ft.
  * **Application Requirements:**
    * Site plan, construction drawings, energy compliance documents
    * Designer of record (if required by local law)


### **Section R106 – Construction Documents**
  * **Contents:**  
Must include:
    * Floor plans
    * Foundation plans
    * Elevations
    * Framing sections
    * Energy calculations (REScheck)
    * Structural details for wind/flood zones
  * **Review:**  
Documents must be reviewed and approved before issuing a permit.


### **Section R107 – Temporary Structures and Uses**
  * Temporary housing or construction offices must be approved and safe for occupancy.
  * Must not interfere with public access or utilities.


### **Section R108 – Fees**
  * Local authorities may charge fees for:
    * Plan review
    * Permit issuance
    * Re-inspections
    * Temporary occupancy certificates
  * **Fee Schedules:**  
Usually based on construction value or square footage.


### **Section R109 – Inspections**
Inspections must be completed at critical stages:
  1. Footing/foundation
  2. Framing
  3. Rough-in (mechanical, electrical, plumbing)
  4. Insulation
  5. Final


The building official may request **third-party testing reports** , especially in wind or flood hazard areas.
### **Section R110 – Certificate of Occupancy**
  * No home can be legally occupied without this certificate.
  * Verifies that all systems are installed safely and to code.


### **Section R111 – Service Utilities**
  * The official may authorize connection or disconnection of utilities (e.g., electric/gas) to protect public safety.


### **Section R112 – Board of Appeals**
  * If a permit is denied or an order is disputed, the applicant has the right to appeal to a local **Board of Appeals**.
  * Ensures **due process** for homeowners, developers, and contractors.


### **Section R113 – Violations**
  * Lists penalties for building without a permit or violating code provisions.
  * Can include fines, legal action, or stop-work orders.


### **Section R114 – Stop Work Order**
  * Issued when construction is unsafe, unauthorized, or deviates from approved plans.
  * Work must cease until the issue is resolved.


## Summary Table
Section | Key Focus  
---|---  
R101 | Scope and applicability of the code  
R102 | Relationship with other laws and referenced codes  
R103–104 | Roles and authority of the Building Official  
R105–106 | Permitting and construction documents  
R107–108 | Temporary uses and fee structures  
R109–110 | Inspection stages and occupancy certification  
R111–114 | Utilities, appeals, violations, and enforcement  
### **Chapter 2 – Definitions**
### **Overview**
Chapter 2 of the IRC is fundamental—it provides the **official definitions** for technical terms used throughout the residential code. These terms standardize how designers, builders, code officials, and inspectors interpret key requirements in planning, permitting, construction, and inspections.
When a term is **defined here** , its use in the rest of the code must align with that exact meaning. If a term is **not defined** in Chapter 2, its meaning should be interpreted using **its common dictionary usage** or **context from related standards** like NFPA, ASTM, or ASCE.
### **How Chapter 2 Works**
  * Chapter 2 includes **terms and definitions** specifically relevant to **residential construction** , such as wall assemblies, load types, energy efficiency, fire resistance, egress paths, and accessibility.
  * This chapter helps enforce the code **consistently** , reducing ambiguity or interpretation disputes.


## **Structure of Chapter 2 Definitions**
Definitions are listed **alphabetically** and grouped by relevance. Here’s a breakdown of the **key categories** of definitions used in the Mississippi Residential Code 2024:
### 1. **Building Elements & Structural Terms**
These terms help clarify the layout, design, and framing elements of homes:
  * **Attic:** The unfinished space between the ceiling joists of the top floor and the roof rafters.
  * **Basement:** The portion of a building that is partly or completely below grade.
  * **Bearing Wall:** A wall that supports vertical loads in addition to its own weight.
  * **Cantilever:** A projecting structure supported only at one end.
  * **Girder:** The main horizontal support beam for a structure.


### 2. **Environmental & Energy Terms**
Used in Chapter 11 (Energy Efficiency):
  * **Building Thermal Envelope:** The system of building components that enclose conditioned space (roof, walls, windows, doors).
  * **Continuous Insulation (CI):** Insulation installed without thermal bridges, across all structural members.
  * **U-Factor:** The rate at which heat flows through a building component. Lower = better insulation.
  * **SHGC (Solar Heat Gain Coefficient):** Measures solar radiation admitted through windows.


### 3. **Fire Safety and Separation Terms**
Used in wall and floor assembly design and fire-resistant construction:
  * **Fireblocking:** Materials installed to resist the free passage of flame and hot gases in concealed spaces.
  * **Draftstopping:** Material to limit the movement of air in horizontal assemblies (like floors).
  * **Fire Separation Distance:** The distance between a building and the property line, other buildings, or centerline of streets.
  * **Townhouse:** A single-family dwelling unit constructed in a group of attached units with property lines between each unit and 2-hour fire-resistance-rated separation walls.


### 4. **Means of Egress Terms**
Used to ensure safe exits from homes:
  * **Means of Egress:** A continuous path of travel from any point in the building to the outside.
  * **Egress Window:** A window required in basements and sleeping rooms for emergency escape.
  * **Exit Access & Exit Discharge:** The portion of egress leading to the exterior and from the exit to the public way.


### 5. **MEP (Mechanical, Electrical, Plumbing) Terms**
Used in Chapters 12–43:
  * **Plenum:** An air compartment in a duct system used for air circulation.
  * **Backflow Preventer:** A device that prevents contaminated water from flowing back into clean water supply lines.
  * **GFCI (Ground-Fault Circuit Interrupter):** A safety device that cuts off power in the event of current leakage, required near wet areas.
  * **Combustion Air:** Air needed to support the combustion of fuel-burning appliances.


### 6. **Load and Design Criteria Terms**
Used for structural design under Chapter 3 and Chapter 8:
  * **Dead Load:** The static weight of building materials and permanent fixtures.
  * **Live Load:** Variable forces from occupancy or use (people, furniture, etc.).
  * **Wind Load:** Pressure exerted by wind based on wind speed and exposure category.
  * **Flood Hazard Area:** Areas subject to flooding as defined by FEMA and local ordinances.


### 7.**Framing and Assembly Terms**
Used throughout Chapters 5, 6, and 7:
  * **Header:** A beam placed over openings like doors or windows to support loads.
  * **Sole Plate:** The bottom horizontal framing member of a wall.
  * **Ridge Board:** The horizontal board at the top of a sloped roof that rafters connect to.
  * **Sheathing:** A board or panel material used in wall, floor, or roof assemblies beneath the finished surface.


### 8. **Occupancy and Use Terms**
  * **Dwelling Unit:** A single unit providing complete living facilities (sleeping, cooking, bathing).
  * **Accessory Structure:** A structure not used for living but used in support of a dwelling (e.g., shed, garage).
  * **Habitable Room:** A room used for living, sleeping, eating, or cooking—not bathrooms, closets, hallways.


* * *
## **Important Notes**
Feature | Detail  
---|---  
**Legal Use** | These definitions carry legal weight in inspections, permits, and dispute resolutions.  
**Referenced Standards** | Definitions often refer to NFPA, ASTM, ASCE, ICC A117.1, and other standards.  
**Mississippi-Specific Usage** | Chapter 2 follows the national IRC 2021 standard. Local jurisdictions may interpret some terms with added guidance (e.g., flood hazard definitions).  
**Cross-Referenced Chapters** | Many defined terms directly impact Chapters 3–10 (planning, egress, fire-resistance, energy).

Maureen Guttman
AIA

# Our Staff
_BCAP’s team, headquartered in Washington, DC, is made up of a diverse group of policy and technical professionals who understand the many complexities of building energy codes._
!Maureen Guttman
**Maureen Guttman, AIA**  
President  
Washington, DC  
(202) 530-2211  
Email Maureen
Maureen is a licensed architect with over twenty-five years of experience in energy efficient and green building design. As BCAP’s President, Maureen oversees a nationwide campaign to support the development and adoption of current model energy codes. In addition, she directs several technical, outreach, and advocacy projects to help various stakeholder groups realize the benefits of energy codes and efficient buildings. Maureen has served on several energy and green construction code development boards, including the development committee for the International Green Construction Code, and this year will serve on the Public Hearing Committee for the Commercial Energy Code. In 2010 Maureen was awarded the President’s Award by the Pennsylvania Chapter of the American Institute of Architects in recognition of a career dedicated to advancing building code adoption and enforcement, and incorporation of sustainability into regulated building practices. Maureen holds a bachelor’s degree in architecture from Carnegie Mellon University.

**Cosimina Panetti**

Albany, NY  
(518) 357-0602  
Email Cosimina
Cosimina joined BCAP over ten years ago supporting the organization’s nationally recognized efforts to assist states and local jurisdictions in enacting and implementing strong and effective building energy codes through advocacy, technical assistance, outreach programs and coalition building efforts. She is a Certified Energy Manager through the Association of Energy Engineers, a LEED AP, and a graduate of the State University of New York Institute of Technology. Prior to joining BCAP, Cosimina spent over 20 years working in different aspects of energy engineering in both the private and public sectors. Working for BCAP, Cosimina has given numerous educational workshops, testimonials and energy code advocacy presentations all over the country. Her work rallying and supporting public and private stakeholders has brought an awareness of the existing regional and local viewpoints that must be accounted for in any successful advocacy campaign. Cosimina has worked effectively with these varying perspectives that exist in the country, managing to build consensus which has resulted in far-reaching acceptance and awareness of the value of energy efficient building codes as good public policy.

Prior to this, she directed a national public awareness campaign in partnership with the Ad Council that garnered more than $140 million in donated and earned media via TV, radio, print, Internet and billboards ads. # Natron’s Collapse Shocks U.S. Battery Ambitions — But China’s Sodium-Ion Revolution Is Just Beginning
When California-based **Natron Energy** closed its doors last month, the headlines sounded like a death knell for sodium-ion batteries in America.
After all, Natron wasn’t just another startup — it was the company that promised a **$1.4 billion battery factory** in North Carolina, aiming to produce up to **14 gigawatt-hours** of next-generation energy storage.
But insiders say: _don’t count sodium-ion out just yet._
While Natron’s failure highlights the U.S.’s uphill battle in clean battery manufacturing, **China is already sprinting ahead** , turning sodium-ion batteries into the next big thing in the energy race.
## Why Natron’s Fall Caught Everyone Off Guard
Founded in 2012, Natron Energy was one of America’s brightest hopes in the sodium-ion space. Its big innovation? Using **Prussian Blue** , a pigment better known for its deep-blue color, to build **both the anode and cathode** of its batteries.
This unique chemistry made Natron’s cells safer, faster to charge, and cheaper to produce than traditional lithium-ion options — perfect for **data centers, grid storage, and EV charging stations** where safety and cost outweigh raw energy density.
In 2023, Natron’s systems hit the market. By 2024, it had opened the **first commercial sodium-ion battery factory in the U.S.** in Michigan, backed by a **$19.8 million ARPA-E grant**.
Then, seemingly overnight, it all collapsed.  
On **September 3, 2025** , Natron ceased operations — its facilities shuttered and emails redirected to **Sherwood Partners** , a liquidation firm.
So, what went wrong?
## Funding Dried Up Before Innovation Could Catch Up
!NATRON ENERGY SHUTDOWN
According to **K.M. Abraham** , CTO of E-KEM Sciences, U.S. startups like Natron often rely on **short funding cycles and investor patience** — two things hard to sustain in deep-tech industries.
> “Companies can’t progress fast enough to satisfy investors,” Abraham explains. “And when milestones slip, funding disappears.”
**Scaling low-energy-density batteries** like Natron’s Prussian Blue models is capital intensive. Building gigawatt-hour-level capacity requires **more lines, more labor, and more capital** — something even multi-billion-dollar lithium-ion players struggle with.
“The math doesn’t lie,” says **Tyler Evans** , CEO of **Mana Battery** , another U.S. sodium-ion startup. “If your cells store less energy, you need more of them — and that means more factories and higher costs.”
## Timing, Not Technology, May Have Been the Real Problem
**Adrian Yao**, from Stanford’s **STEER initiative** , says Natron’s concept was ahead of its market.
> “Hyperscalers are racing to build data centers, not rethink backup power systems,” Yao notes. “Natron may have just been early to the game.”
He’s not alone. Earlier this year, another sodium-ion hopeful, **Bedrock Materials** , also folded.
“The battery business is brutal,” says **Andrew Thomas** , cofounder of **Acculon Energy**. “There are a lot of tombstones in the field.”
##  Meanwhile in China, Sodium-Ion Is Exploding
While the West debates and defunds, **China builds**.
The **International Energy Agency (IEA)** estimates that China produces **over 75% of the world’s batteries** — and it’s quickly extending that dominance to sodium-ion.
Battery giant **CATL** already launched **Naxtra** , its second-generation sodium-ion battery brand, designed for **electric vehicles** and **energy storage systems**.
Chinese manufacturers are doubling down on large-scale sodium-ion applications — and unlike the U.S., they’re not struggling with manufacturing yield or workforce readiness.
> “The West focuses too much on innovation and not enough on making things,” Yao warns. “China’s yield rates and manufacturing expertise are unmatched.”
## What’s Next for the Sodium-Ion Industry?
Despite Natron’s fall, the U.S. sodium-ion dream isn’t dead.
Startups like **Mana Battery** and **Acculon Energy** are learning from China’s playbook — **partnering with existing manufacturers** to scale production faster and cheaper.
Evans believes this hybrid approach could finally put the U.S. back in the race.
> “It’s a commercialization sweet spot unique to sodium,” he says. “The market for grid storage, data centers, and low-cost mobility is just opening.”
As global demand for **safe, affordable, non-lithium energy storage** skyrockets, sodium-ion may still emerge as a crucial player in the clean-energy transition — just not under Natron’s banner.
## The Bottom Line
Natron’s closure isn’t the end of sodium-ion batteries — it’s a reality check.  
The message is clear: **innovation alone isn’t enough.**  
Manufacturing strength, supply chain readiness, and strategic patience are the new power plays in the global battery race.
China got that memo years ago.  
Now, it’s up to the U.S. to catch up — or risk watching the next energy revolution happen from the sidelines.
!mg author box
Maureen Guttman
AIA  
President  
Washington, DC

# Top Sodium-Ion Battery Companies in 2025: The Future of Energy Storage
With the **global push for sustainable energy** , sodium-ion batteries are emerging as a **cost-effective, safe, and scalable** alternative to lithium-ion technology. Leading battery manufacturers are developing **next-generation sodium-ion solutions** for applications ranging from **home energy storage to grid-scale deployment**.
!sodium ion batteries market share Technavio has announced its latest market research report titled Global Sodium Ion Battery Market 2024-2028
This article ranks the **top sodium-ion battery companies** revolutionizing the industry and compares their innovations.
**Learn More About Sodium-Ion Technology:**
  * Sodium-Ion vs. Lithium-Ion Batteries
  * [Industrial Applications of Sodium-Ion Batteries](https://bcapcodes.org/sodium-ion/industrial-applications-of-sodium-ion-batteries/)
  * Future of Sodium-Ion Batteries


## **Comparison Table: Leading Sodium-Ion Battery Manufacturers (2025)**
**Company** | **Founded** | **HQ Location** | **Key Innovations** | **Primary Applications**  
---|---|---|---|---  
**NextThing Tech** | 2019 | USA | Scalable sodium-ion home & commercial energy storage | Residential, Utility-Scale Storage, EV Charging  
**Faradion** | 2011 | UK | First to commercialize sodium-ion technology | EVs, Grid Storage  
**AMTE Power** | 1997 | UK | Gigafactory development for large-scale production | Renewable Storage, Automotive  
**Northvolt** | 2016 | Sweden | Sustainable sodium-ion battery solutions | Energy Storage  
**Natron Energy** | 2012 | USA | Prussian Blue chemistry for ultra-fast charging | Data Centers, Industrial Backup  
**Altris** | 2017 | Sweden | High energy density & eco-friendly battery chemistry | Stationary Energy Storage  
**HiNa Battery** | 2017 | China | First large-scale sodium-ion battery production in China | EVs, Industrial Applications  
* * *
## **1. NextThing Technologies: The Future of Home & Utility-Scale Sodium-Ion Batteries!next thing technologies **
**Website**
### **Overview**
NextThing Technologies is **pioneering a sodium-ion battery system** designed for **home energy storage, commercial use, and grid-scale applications**. The company’s focus is on making energy storage **safer, more affordable, scalable, and eco-friendly** than lithium-ion options.
### **Key Innovations**
!✅ **Home-Ready Sodium-Ion Battery Packs** – Successfully developed and tested working prototypes.  
!✅ **Scalable Energy Storage** – Designed to store electricity **from solar panels or off-peak grid power** for use during peak hours or power outages.  
!✅ **Industrial & Grid-Scale Applications** – Aims to **support renewable energy integration** into the grid.  
!✅ **Potential Use Cases:**
  * **Residential Storage:** Energy independence for homeowners.
  * **Commercial & Industrial Energy Storage:** Reduces business energy costs.
  * **Data Centers:** Ensures reliable backup power.
  * !⚡ **EV Charging Stations:** Helps manage peak loads and enables fast charging.
  * **Off-Grid Power Systems:** Reliable energy storage for remote areas.


### **Future Plans**
Currently refining its **battery design & production process**.  
Seeking **additional funding to accelerate market entry**.  
Plans for **pilot production and large-scale manufacturing**.
NextThing Technologie is **positioned to revolutionize the energy storage industry** with its **innovative sodium-ion solutions**.
## **2. Faradion: Pioneering Sodium-Ion Technology**
!top sodium ion batteries 
**Website**
**Overview:** A UK-based leader in sodium-ion technology, **Faradion** was the **first company to commercialize sodium-ion batteries**. Now backed by **Reliance Industries** , it is scaling up global production.
### **Key Highlights:**
!✅ High-energy-density sodium-ion batteries for **EVs & grid storage**.  
!✅ **Stronger safety profile** compared to lithium-ion.  
!✅ **Targeting electric vehicles, energy storage, and commercial backup power.**
## **3. AMTE Power: Scaling Up Sodium-Ion Manufacturing**
!amte power 
**Website**
**Overview:** AMTE Power is focused on bringing sodium-ion technology to **commercial markets, renewable energy storage, and automotive applications**.
### **Key Highlights:**
!✅ First European company with **UN38.3 certified sodium-ion cells**.  
!✅ Plans to **open a sodium-ion Gigafactory in Dundee by 2027**.  
!✅ Strong partnerships with **Formula 1 teams & major OEMs**.
## **4. Northvolt: Sustainable Sodium-Ion Battery Solutions**
!best sodium ion batteries company
**Website**
**Overview:** Northvolt is a major European energy company investing in **scalable, eco-friendly sodium-ion batteries**.
### **Key Highlights:**
!✅ Developing **low-cost, high-efficiency storage solutions**.  
!✅ Partnerships with **leading European energy firms**.  
!✅ Focused on **stationary and industrial energy storage applications**.
## **5. Natron Energy**
!best sodium ion batteries
**Website**
**Overview:** Natron Energy specializes in **fast-charging sodium-ion batteries using Prussian Blue chemistry** , which offer **exceptional safety and long lifespan**.
### **Key Highlights:**
!✅ **50,000+ cycle life** – ideal for industrial backup power.  
!✅ **Ultra-fast charging technology**.  
!✅ Used in **data centers, telecom backup, and energy storage**.
## **6. Altris**
!award winning sodium ion batteries companies 
**Website**
**Overview:** Altris is developing **high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries** , perfect for **renewable energy storage applications**.
### **Key Highlights:**
!✅ 3 GWh sodium-ion **battery factory in Sweden**.  
!✅ Uses **Prussian White cathode materials** for sustainability.  
!✅ Targeting **grid storage and industrial applications**.
## **7. HiNa Battery: China’s Sodium-Ion Battery Pioneer**
![](https://bcapcodes.org/sodium-ion/top-sodiumion-battery-companies/attachment/screenshot-2025-02-11-121137/)
**Website**
**Overview:** HiNa Battery is **China’s first large-scale sodium-ion battery producer** , currently working on **GWh-scale production** for **EV and industrial applications**.
### **Key Highlights:**
!✅ First **mass-produced sodium-ion battery in China**.  
!✅ Collaborating with **JAC Motors** for sodium-ion EV trials.  
!✅ Targeting **low-speed EVs, industrial storage, and commercial power backup**.
## **Why Sodium-Ion Batteries Are the Future**
!✅ **Abundant & Cost-Effective** – Sodium is **1,000x more abundant** than lithium, reducing material costs.  
!✅ **Higher Safety Standards** – **No risk of thermal runaway** , unlike lithium-ion.  
!✅ **Longer Lifespan** – Many sodium-ion batteries exceed **50,000+ cycles**.  
!✅ **Better for Renewable Energy Storage** – Ideal for **grid backup and solar integration**.
**Explore More About Sodium-Ion Batteries:**
  * Sodium-Ion vs. Lithium-Ion Batteries
  * [Industrial Applications of Sodium-Ion Batteries](https://bcapcodes.org/sodium-ion/industrial-applications-of-sodium-ion-batteries/)
  * Future of Sodium-Ion Batteries


## **Conclusion: Sodium-Ion Batteries Are Shaping the Future of Energy Storage**
With companies like **NextThing Technologies, Faradion, AMTE Power, and Natron Energy** leading advancements, **sodium-ion technology is set to redefine energy storage**. The industry is moving toward **scalable, safe, and cost-efficient battery solutions** , making sodium-ion batteries a **cornerstone of future energy infrastructure**.

# Homebuyer Demand for Energy Codes
Consumer demand for energy efficiency is a topic energy code advocates need to understand. We want to know the answers to questions like “do consumers believe in conserving energy through increasing energy efficiency in their homes?” and “how much are consumers willing to pay for home improvements for efficiency?” so that we can make a stronger case for our support for energy efficient building codes. Recently, BCAP looked at four major consumer surveys and summarized their findings in a fact sheet. Although the surveys were conducted by various organizations, the findings led to a strikingly similar conclusion:
**Consumers want and expect energy efficiency when buying a new home**.
You can read the full reports of each of the surveys at the following links:
  * What Home Buyers Really Want
  * [Energy Codes Messaging Test](https://bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/12/Energy-Codes-Messaging-Test.pdf)
  * [Idaho Homeowner Energy Code Survey](http://www.idahoenergycode.com/sites/idahoenergycode.com/files/resources/pdf/BPA%20Grant%20Idaho%20Energy%20Code%20Survey%2031May2014.pdf)
  * [2015 Public Opinion Survey](http://www.ndare.org/vertical/sites/%7BE50B81F9-F226-4A80-AE75-3CBA3D28BCB4%7D/uploads/ND_Alliance_for_Renewable_Energy_Report_%28REVISED_Mar_5_2015%29.pdf)


#####  [Click here to read the fact sheet](https://bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Homebuyer-Demand-Four-Factsheets.pdf).
!Kamaria Greenfield
Kamaria Greenfield

# Tools
##### BCAP offers a number of useful tools to support state and local governments, advocates, industry, and inspection professionals, and other stakeholders.
##### Incremental Cost Analysis
* * *
BCAP challenges construction cost as a barrier to energy code adoption with a quantitative analysis of the incremental cost of upgrading to the 2012 IECC.
Please also see our 2009 IECC analysis.
##### Adoption Maps
* * *
![](https://www.bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/08/national_map_7aab5a_2-01.png)
BCAP has created a series of maps to provide a national snapshot of building energy code adoption and implementation status. These commercial and residential maps are intended to keep stakeholders up-to-date.
##### Policy Action Toolkit
* * *
![](https://bcapcodes.org/tools/)
Assistance for state and local governments to adopt or update energy codes and above-code programs through policy guidance and sample legislation. This toolkit includes a summary of the current model codes, strategies for communicating with various stakeholders, and the variation of energy code policies and adoption methods from state to state.
##### Local Implementation Kit
* * *
![](https://www.bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/08/climate-zone-map-e1451582242608.png)
Your state has adopted a new energy code. Now what? This page provides a step-by-step overview to implementation of a new state code at the local level with helpful links and resources. A new or updated energy code can be a large undertaking, and proper implementation at the jurisdictional level is crucial to realizing the potential energy cost and emissions reductions.
##### Code Builder
* * *
![](https://www.bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/08/code_builder_res_12292015-01-300x213-1.jpg)
These revamped pages are designed to explain how each material component of the energy code fits into the overall organization of a home or building. Click on either of the items below to learn how these component work, both individually and together, to make our built environment more energy efficient.

  * Privacy Policy

# California 2010 ADA Standards
**Adoption Info of California 2010 ADA Standards**
**Official title** 2010 ADA Standards for Accessible Design
**Effective dates** March 15, 2012 – Present
**Adopts with amendments** 2010 ADA Standards for Accessible Design  
Read More: California Standard for Residential Construction in High Wind Regions 2014
California Standard for Residential Construction in High Wind Regions 2020
**Overview** The 2010 ADA Standards for Accessible Design are federal standards that set the minimum requirements for creating accessible facilities and spaces to ensure equal access for individuals with disabilities in commercial facilities, public accommodations, and state and local government facilities. These standards provide guidelines for accessible routes, building elements, restrooms, and communication features to ensure accessibility for all users.
California adopts the 2010 ADA Standards with specific amendments to address state-specific requirements and enhance accessibility. These modifications ensure that facilities in California meet or exceed the federal standards, providing safe and accessible environments for individuals with disabilities.
!mg author box
Maureen Guttman
AIA  
President  
Washington, DC

# California OSHA 1926 Construction
**Adoption Info of California OSHA 1926 Construction Standards**
**Official title** California OSHA 1926 Construction Standards
**Effective dates** Current edition (reflecting the latest updates)
**Published by** California Division of Occupational Safety and Health (Cal/OSHA)  
Read More: California Fair Housing Act Design Manual
California Aluminum and Aluminum Alloy Steel and Plate Code 2014
**Overview** The California OSHA 1926 Construction Standards provide comprehensive safety and health regulations for construction activities in California. These standards, based on the federal OSHA regulations found in 29 CFR Part 1926, include specific requirements for construction site safety, health programs, equipment, and procedures to protect workers from occupational hazards.
Key areas covered include general safety and health provisions, fire protection and prevention, personal protective equipment, fall protection, scaffolding, excavation safety, and electrical safety. The standards are regularly updated to incorporate the latest safety practices and technological advancements.
The California OSHA 1926 Construction Standards are essential for contractors, construction managers, safety professionals, and workers to ensure compliance with state-specific regulations and to maintain a safe and healthy work environment on construction sites.
!mg author box
Maureen Guttman
AIA  
President  
Washington, DC

# Who We Are
##### The Building Codes Assistance Project (BCAP) is a nonprofit advocacy organization that promotes the adoption, implementation and advancement of building energy codes on the state, local, and international levels.
With over twenty years of experience, BCAP has established itself as a trusted, non-partisan U.S. leader for energy code advocacy, research and analysis, technical support, training, and code status tracking. From its national platform, BCAP facilitates increased communication and collaboration between allies, identifies and navigates past policy and structural pitfalls, and helps state and local decision-makers design strategies to improve building energy efficiency.
#### History and Mission
The Building Codes Assistance Project (BCAP) was established in 1994 as a joint initiative of the Alliance to Save Energy, the American Council for an Energy-Efficient Economy, and the Natural Resources Defense Council. On June 1, 2014, BCAP “graduated” from its status as a program of the Alliance and became an independent organization and a proud project of the Trust for Conservation Innovation. BCAP is headquartered in Washington, DC. We have satellite staff in Albany, NY and Toledo, OH.
BCAP’s goal is to reduce the energy consumed in the construction and operation of buildings by working with national, state, and local governments and other international and national stakeholders to promote the adoption, enforcement, and compliance of building energy codes and standards. BCAP aspires to be the premier resource for energy code support, coordination, technical assistance, news, and information.
**Interested in supporting our work?** Your donation, less goods or services received, is tax deductible to fullest extent of the law. Your financial contributions are very much needed to help us carry out our vision.
!DonateButton
  
![](https://www.bcapcodes.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/08/aceee-logo-1.png)
#### Selected Projects
  * Compliance Planning Assistance  
Since 2010, BCAP has worked with 22 states through the CPA Program to help them take practical steps towards achieving full compliance with the model energy codes. The CPA Program is divided into two phases:
    * **Gap Analysis Report** : Comprehensive document of the state’s existing energy code infrastructure that assesses the gaps between the state and its compliance goals, identifies best practices, and offer initial recommendations for improvement.
    * **Strategic Compliance Plan** : A targeted, state-specific road map with practical near- and long-term action items based on Gap Analysis recommendations to move the state towards full energy code compliance.
  * Consumer Outreach Campaign  
Everyone has a right to a home that meets or exceeds national standards. That’s why BCAP teamed up with the Consumers Union—makers of Consumer Reports—to educate the public on the impact of home energy use, the role of energy codes in addressing home energy performance, and what you can do as a consumer to save energy—and money. BCAP has launched campaigns in several states and cities – to raise consumer awareness and support of energy codes. BCAP and Consumers Union created a national energy code checklist and guide, in addition to state-specific resources.For information on our Consumer’s campaign, please visit our Consumers Portal or visit the Consumers Union Build Better, Live Better campaign.
  * Incremental Cost Analysis  
One of the major barriers to energy code adoption is the concern that the cost of upgrading homes to comply with the latest model energy code would be prohibitive. To address this issue for policymakers and the building community, BCAP completed a nationwide incremental cost analysis (as well as on demand studies for states and cities). Each report and accompanying handout provides a clear, conservative estimate of the added upfront construction costs, the monthly energy bill savings, and long-term homeowner profit from the energy code upgrade specific to each state and city studied.
  * [Energy Code Ambassador Program](https://www.bcapcodes.org/projects/ecap/)  
BCAP and the International Code Council (ICC) created this initiative to empower existing code professionals so they may develop their familiarity and expertise with energy codes and share their knowledge throughout their jurisdiction and state.


#### Our Staff
A team of policy and technical professionals who understand the complexity of energy codes
* * *
#### Our Services
BCAP provides a range of services to federal, state and local governments, foundations, private organizations and other nonprofits.
  * Adoption Support
  * Compliance Assistance
  * Technical Expertise
  * Outreach and Coordination
  * International Work


* * *
#### Annual Reports
BCAP Annual Reports include the status of the organization and codes at the time of publication, summaries of projects from the past and upcoming years, updated services, financial information, and more.

###  [ Homebuyer Demand for Energy Codes ](https://bcapcodes.org/survey/homebuyer-demand-for-energy-codes/)
Consumer demand for energy efficiency is a topic energy code advocates need to understand. We want to know the answers to questions like “do consumers believe in conserving energy through increasing energy efficiency in their homes?”
